The $10 Billion Hunt for the Rocks That Power the World

Bloomberg Originals(5.0M)·12:13·69.3K views·2026-05-15
Donald TrumpUS President Xi JinpingPresident of China
TL;DRChina’s near-total monopoly on the rare earth supply chain (80-90%) has become a critical geopolitical "bazooka," forcing a $10 billion global scramble led by the U.S.
and allies to secure independent mining, processing, and magnet manufacturing. The 2025 Trade War:...

TL;DR

China’s near-total monopoly on the rare earth supply chain (80-90%) has become a critical geopolitical "bazooka," forcing a $10 billion global scramble led by the U.S. and allies to secure independent mining, processing, and magnet manufacturing.

The Geopolitical "Rare Earth Bazooka"

  • The 2025 Trade War: Following the return of Donald Trump to the White House and the imposition of 145% tariffs on Chinese goods, Beijing retaliated with export controls on rare earth elements.
  • Economic Vulnerability: The U.S. automotive industry warned of total factory shutdowns if permanent magnets—essential for electric motors—remained blocked by Chinese controls.
  • Historical Leverage: This mirrors the 2010 territorial dispute with Japan, where China restricted exports to cripple the Japanese automotive sector, providing an early warning of the risks of single-source dependency.

The Processing and Manufacturing Chokepoint

  • Control Beyond Mining: While China holds the largest deposits, its true dominance lies in downstream processing.
      • Refining: China controls 90% of the chemical separation and refining process.
      • Magnet Production: China controls roughly 94% of the global production of rare earth permanent magnets.
  • Environmental Outsourcing: Western nations historically ceded this industry to China due to the highly toxic and radioactive waste produced during the refining stage.
  • The Talent Gap: A significant barrier to Western competition is specialized labor; China has dedicated universities for mining and engineering with 25,000 students, while the U.S. graduated only 350 mining engineers last year.

National Security and the Iran War

  • Depleted Munitions: The U.S. military has rapidly consumed its stockpiles of advanced weapon systems during the Iran War, using more than half of its pre-war inventory of certain high-cost munitions.
  • Defense Necessity: Rare earths are essential for the guidance systems and sensors in these weapons, making the replenishment of stockpiles impossible without a secure supply chain.
  • Strategic Red Lines: China has specifically banned the export of rare earths for use in U.S. weapons, labeling their use in defense as an "illegal diversion."

The $10 Billion Global Pivot

  • Western Investment: An estimated $10 billion in public funds will be invested in non-Chinese rare earth ventures in 2026 to "backstop" companies against Chinese price manipulation.
  • Key Market Players:
      • Lynas (Australia): The leading producer outside China; it recently broke the monopoly on "heavy" rare earths and holds a $96 million U.S. defense contract.
      • MP Materials (USA): The Pentagon took a $400 million stake in this company in 2025 to accelerate domestic heavy rare earth separation.
      • Meteoric Resources (Brazil): Developing a 1.5-billion-ton rare earth clay deposit, positioning Brazil as a major sustainable alternative to Chinese ore.
  • Long-term Outlook: Experts estimate that while the monopoly won't be broken in five years, the goal of a 50/50 supply split between China and the West is achievable by the early 2030s.

Notable Quotes

"The Middle East has its oil, but China has its rare earth." — Deng Xiaoping, Former Paramount Leader of China
"We broke the monopoly on separated heavy rare earths last year in May 2025. We are the only producer at scale of both separated lights and separated heavies outside of China." — Representative, Lynas
"The automotive industry came out and not so quietly said we are going to have to shut down factories if we cannot get these permanent magnets." — Bloomberg Narrative
"This is a story about not letting one country hold all the chips on what is a very important supply chain." — Bloomberg Narrative
It doesn't look like much, but this is the prize for the rest of the world in trying to catch up with China. We need rare earths. China owns them, processes them, refines them, and sells them. The entire supply chain is built around Beijing. No matter what you do, you're always kind of beholden to China. Rare earths are really the backbone of modern technology. China's the ballgame. They're 80 to 90% of production and processing of rare earths that go into all equipment around the world. And China's shown it will use that dominance as geopolitical leverage. The main use of rare earths are in these powerful high-performance magnets. China's capacity for magnets is vast compared to the rest of the world. When Donald Trump returned to the White House in 2025 and rebooted the US-China trade war, China responded with export controls on rare earths that exposed just how much Western manufacturing still depends on China's supply. The automotive industry came out and not so quietly said we are going to have to shut down factories if we cannot get these permanent magnets. That shock set off a broader scramble to build a new supply chain from mine to magnet. This is a story about not letting one country hold all the chips on what is a very important supply chain. The US-China trade war has begun. In case you'd forgotten how influential rare earths are, it was little more than a year ago that they forced a pause in the US-China trade war. We have a 145% tariff on Chinese goods; neither one wants to be the one to pick up the phone first. In April 2025, US President Donald Trump slapped huge tariffs on Chinese imports. Beijing fought back, pulling out its rare earth bazooka. The automotive CEOs from Detroit were all showing up, telling Donald Trump that if you do not get those permanent magnets, which are made from the rare earths, moving back into the United States, we're going to have to start shutting factories. Beijing had located the weak spot, and at the center of it was a group of metals most people never think about. Rare earths are a group of 17 metallic elements that are chemically very similar. They can seem obscure, but the important thing is what they do. They help create powerful, heat-resistant magnets. They're used in, you know, most of our modern technologies, especially in electric motors. They make our gadgets smaller, lighter and more powerful. From phones and wind turbines to electric vehicles and missile systems, rare earth magnets sit inside much of modern technology. And that means the global high-tech economy relies on one country. China's home to the world's largest rare earth deposit that's been discovered so far. Specifically, it has deposits of heavy rare earths, whereas the US has very, very little. Heavy rare earths are the most expensive and sought after. They allow high-performance magnets to resist heat and demagnetization. The real choke point is the processing stage. China controls 90% of the refining process, and then it goes to magnet production, which is the process of making rare earth metals into magnets. China controls about 94% of that as well. Mining the ore is only the start. The real work is in the chemistry, separating and refining each element into something magnet makers can use. The production is so costly and also environmentally very damaging because the processing stage, you know, produces a lot of toxic waste including radioactive materials. So a lot of Western countries, they're happy to let China take over. And you can see why. This is Baotou in Inner Mongolia, home to China's largest rare earth production site. Wherever you are in the world, at some level of that supply chain, you are probably reliant on Chinese products. With China holding such a dominant position, why did it come as such a shock to the Trump administration when they chose to use it? It really shouldn't have been, because China used the exact leverage 15 years ago. Perhaps because last time it wasn't the US in the crosshairs; it was Japan. China first used its dominance over rare earths as a geopolitical weapon in 2010 when they restricted exports to Japan because of a territorial dispute. China said, okay, we are going to restrict the exports of our rare earths that you need, Japan, to turn into the permanent magnets that go into your automotive industry and other industries. This was the first kind of shock to the market. It lasted only weeks. Even so, it exposed Japan's vulnerability and offered an early warning of how much leverage China had built. To understand how that happened, we have to rewind. Actually, the US was the biggest player in rare earths all the way from the 1950s to the 1980s. China is a latecomer in the industry, but it caught up really fast. This was not an accident. Beijing treated rare earths as a strategic industry and built capacity all the way down the chain. China's late paramount leader Deng Xiaoping had said famously that the Middle East has its oil, but China has its rare earth. As China scaled up, it became cheaper to buy from there than to make it anywhere else. In the West, thin margins, high labor costs and dirty processes pushed many operators out. Consumers were happy with the status quo because they got a steady flow of cheap, reliable supplies of magnets from China for many, many years. But now the risks of relying so much on China are clear, forcing countries to make their own rare earth plays. This is the largest rare earth processing site outside of China. It's run by the Australian mining company Lynas, which has a 96 million dollar contract to supply heavy rare earths to the US Department of Defense. Lynas is really the leader in the wave of companies now trying to rebuild the rare earth supply chain outside of China. They started more than a decade ago; it took them a long time to start ramping up production. Lynas aims to produce over 10,000 tons of rare earth oxides per year from this site. But most importantly, it's worked out how to produce heavy rare earths. We broke the monopoly on separated heavy rare earths last year in May 2025. We are the only producer at scale of both separated lights and separated heavies outside of China. Rebuilding a supply chain this specialized also means rebuilding skills, experience and industrial muscle. Last year in the US, they graduated 35,000 lawyers. They graduated 350 mining engineers. Meanwhile, China has a dedicated mining and engineering university with 25,000 undergraduate students. China has invested in developing competence in the rare earths market and getting better and being more efficient every day. And the urgency for the US has only grown as its war with Iran has depleted the Pentagon's advanced weapon stocks. We've seen the US burning through its stockpiles of defense systems and weapon systems really quickly. And the US will need to replenish these stockpiles, and to make weapons and defense systems, it requires rare earths. The US military's exact demand is classified, but a recent report by the Center for Strategic and International Studies estimates during the Iran war, the US has used more than half of its pre-war inventory of some very expensive munitions. There is definitely a sense of urgency, and it's not just the United States. It is the Western world. If countries want an alternative to China, they need more than new factories. They also need new deposits. And Brazil is one of the main bets. Latin America's biggest economy is home to roughly a quarter of the world's rare earths, according to the US Geological Survey. This site in southeastern Brazil is run by another Australian developer, Meteoric Resources, that's also receiving backing from the US. The company says there are 1.5 billion tons of rare earth clay in these hills. Our main objective right now is to put the project in production, to be the next to the world to produce the mixed rare earth carbonate and to develop the technology to do the separation. And we can do that differently as China does right now. If you want to be sustainable, you have to buy from a sustainable market. And the US is also investing at home. Their biggest hope is the Pentagon-backed MP Materials. The Department of Defense bought a 400 million dollar stake in the company in 2025. It expects to be able to separate heavy rare earths later this year. But the problem for every would-be challenger: China's big enough to squeeze margins long before new competitors are secure. The Western world are intervening and saying that they're willing to put up money to build out these companies, to effectively be a partner that says we're going to backstop any chance that China takes to flush you out of the market. China's restrictions on rare earth exports to the US have been relaxing, but its ban on exports for use in US weapons is a red line. The objective is to prevent the illegal diversion of rare earths toward improper uses, such as weapons of mass destruction. I think the main lesson we can draw is that building up the supply chain takes time and a lot of efforts. Japan's dependence on China only reduced from 90% in 2010 to about 60 to 70% now. So you can tell that the 15-year process, it's long and it's slow. If you're a company that wants 60% from China, 40% from the rest of the world, or 50/50, that's a more realizable goal up to 2030 and into the early next decade. A Bloomberg Intelligence report estimates 10 billion dollars of public funds will be invested in rare earth ventures outside of China in 2026. That's a big turnaround. The monopoly is not going to be broken within the next five years. But the US, Australia, Brazil, Japan, South Korea, France will all begin producing some commercial scale of permanent magnets of rare earths. And if you're sitting here five years from now and those nations are all producing stuff that actually goes out the door to major buyers, then that's a win.

Why This Market “Melt-Up” is Making Me Nervous | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(174.0K)·21:32·58.1K views·2026-05-15
Steve EismanPortfolio Manager & Host
TL;DRSteve Eisman warns that the current market "melt-up" has become dangerously frothy, characterized by extreme concentration in AI-related semiconductors, the disappearance of defensive sectors, and rising inflation data that may threaten the sustainability of recent gains.

TL;DR

Steve Eisman warns that the current market "melt-up" has become dangerously frothy, characterized by extreme concentration in AI-related semiconductors, the disappearance of defensive sectors, and rising inflation data that may threaten the sustainability of recent gains.

Market Overheating and Macro Risks

  • Signs of "Froth": Eisman expresses nervousness regarding the current rally, noting that the S&P 500 is up 9% and the Nasdaq is up 15% in a little over a month.
      • Sector Concentration: Semiconductors now represent over 18% of the S&P 500. Notably, NVIDIA's market cap now exceeds the entire healthcare sector.
      • Vanishing Defensives: The combined weight of the four defensive sectors (Healthcare, Consumer Staples, Energy, and Utilities) has plummeted from 31% at the end of 2022 to just 19% today.
  • Retail Sentiment: Retail traders are at their most bullish since 2021, aggressively buying calls on the "Mag 10" stocks (the Magnificent Seven plus AMD, Palantir, and Broadcom).
  • Inflationary Pressures: The Producer Price Index (PPI) rose 1.4% in April, far exceeding the 0.5% forecast. On an annual basis, PPI is up 6%, the highest since late 2022.
      • Interest Rates: The 10-year yield is flirting with 4.5%, a level Eisman describes as a "Rubicon" for the markets.

Private Credit and "A Conspiracy of Credit"

  • Systemic Risks: Eisman is launching a masterclass focusing on the "veil of secrecy" in private credit. He draws parallels between current private credit growth and the toxic mortgage bonds of the Great Financial Crisis (GFC).
  • FS KKR Capital (FSK) Warning: This public BDC is a "canary in the coal mine."
      • Credit Cuts: JPMorgan and a group of banks slashed FSK’s credit line by $650 million (14%) and raised interest rates on the remainder. Eisman notes that credit line cuts for financial services firms are a major red flag.
      • Loan Markdowns: According to MSCI, private credit funds have already marked down more than 10% of their loans by at least 50%.
  • Outlook: While Eisman expects significant losses and a potential recession in this space, he believes a total disaster is unlikely because banks remain well-capitalized.

Mixed Earnings and Sector Volatility

  • The Payment Space: Eisman describes investing here as "rolling dice" due to hyper-competition.
      • Toast: Dropped 17% following weak EBITDA guidance despite an earnings beat.
      • Bill & Block: Both saw gains after significant workforce reductions (30-40%) to pivot toward AI and profitability, though both remain volatile.
  • AI and Infrastructure:
      • Cisco: A major winner, up double digits after reporting 12% revenue growth and strong AI-related orders.
      • CoreWeave: Despite revenue more than doubling, the stock dropped 8% due to a miss on forward revenue guidance. Eisman notes that "money-losing" companies cannot afford to miss on any metric.
      • Constellation Energy (CEG): Reported 28% growth due to the AI data center boom but saw a negative stock reaction as management left full-year guidance unchanged.
  • Travel and Tech Softness:
      • Expedia: Down 9% due to weak booking forecasts and "geopolitical uncertainty."
      • The Trade Desk: Once a high-flyer, the stock is down 46% this year as revenue growth slowed from 20% to 8%, driven by competition from Amazon.

Individual Positions and Special Situations

  • FICO Short: Eisman remains short on FICO, citing its 1,500% price hikes over five years and its eroding monopoly status as regulators look for cheaper scoring alternatives.
  • Homebuilders: Despite being bearish on FICO, Eisman remains long on Meritage Homes, arguing that buying a homebuilder below tangible book value is a sound long-term strategy, even if rising rates delay the payoff.
  • eBay/GameStop: eBay rejected GameStop’s takeover offer, questioning the financing. Eisman characterized Ryan Cohen’s recent public behavior regarding the offer as "bizarre."

Notable Quotes

"The sum of the four defensive sectors—healthcare, consumer staples, energy, and utilities—represents only 19% of the S&P 500. That number was 31% at the end of 2022. Defensive sectors seem to be disappearing before our eyes." — Steve Eisman
"Almost all the growth in lending since 2008 has been in private credit behind a veil of privacy, which means secrecy... History seems to be repeating itself." — Steve Eisman
"When a financial services company has its credit lines cut, that is always really, really bad." — Steve Eisman
"I have to admit, I am nervous about the sustainability of this melt-up... This week, I lightened up, especially on my high flyers, because this market is feeling bubbly." — Steve Eisman

Three Kinds of Software Survive: Tasklet's Andrew Lee on Competing to be a Horizontal Platform

Cognitive Revolution "How AI Changes Everything"(42.9K)·94:54·19 views·2026-05-15
Andrew LeeCEO, Tasklet
TL;DRTasklet has rewritten its entire agent stack to transition from a workflow automation tool into a horizontal general-purpose platform that acts as a "mecha suit" for multiple frontier models.
This strategic shift allows Tasklet to navigate intense token-cost competition...

TL;DR

Tasklet has rewritten its entire agent stack to transition from a workflow automation tool into a horizontal general-purpose platform that acts as a "mecha suit" for multiple frontier models. This strategic shift allows Tasklet to navigate intense token-cost competition from its primary supplier, Anthropic, and positions the company as one of the few horizontal platforms poised to survive the AI transition.

The Technical Overhaul: File Systems, Computer Use, and Context Compaction

  • File System Context: Tasklet replaced infinitely long chat histories with a file system-based architecture to conserve tokens and reduce costs.
      • Rather than feeding entire conversations into the LLM, the system stores history in the file system and sends the LLM "hints" and pointers on which files to read.
  • Context Compaction: Implemented a bucketed, cache-aware compression system that decreases history fidelity as conversations age.
      • Recent Turns: Maintained at high granularity, including all thinking blocks, tool calls, and raw responses.
      • Older Turns: Progressively stripped of thinking blocks, truncated, and eventually subjected to LLM-based summarization to maximize caching efficiency.
  • Core Computer Use: Computer use has graduated from an experimental add-on to the core of the product.
      • Every active agent now runs persistent, headless browser VMs to execute shell commands, touch databases, and perform actions.
  • Infrastructure Endorsements:
      • BlackSands: Endorsed as Tasklet's primary sandbox VM vendor due to its ultra-fast cold starts and reliable performance.
      • Firecrawl: Utilized for web crawling and scraping.
      • Token Spend: Tasklet currently spends approximately 5% to 10% of its internal payroll budget on development and testing tokens.

The Shift to a Multi-Model, Horizontal Platform

  • Platform Neutrality: Tasklet is moving away from being exclusively built on Anthropic models to support a neutral ecosystem that includes OpenAI, Gemini, DeepSeek, and Kimi.
      • This mitigates supply-chain risks and allows corporate customers to optimize performance and costs dynamically.
  • The Supplier-Competitor Dilemma: Anthropic serves as Tasklet’s primary model provider but also stands as its toughest competitor.
      • Churn Destination: Approximately 80% of users who churn from Tasklet migrate directly to Anthropic's first-party Claude Max accounts.
      • Subsidized Pricing: Anthropic's first-party Claude Max plans provide users with an estimated 5:1 cost advantage in token allocation compared to API pricing.
  • The Tokenizer Cost Barrier: Tasklet opted to stick with Opus 4.6 rather than upgrading to 4.7 due to a new tokenizer that increased API costs by roughly 30%.
  • The "Mecha Suit" Paradigm: Andrew conceptualizes the developer harness not as a constraint on the model, but as a "mecha suit" that equips the LLM with the storage, compute, APIs, and UIs necessary to act on the world.

The Three Surviving Software Categories

Andrew predicts that the shift toward general-purpose AI agents will render traditional, single-purpose SaaS UIs obsolete, leaving only three types of software companies standing:

  • Horizontal Platforms: A very small number of general-purpose AI platforms (such as Tasklet) that serve as the main interface for knowledge workers, managing shared organizational context and workflows.
  • Headless Companies: API-first utility companies (such as Stripe) that manage highly complex, back-end transactional infrastructure (e.g., cross-border payments, compliance) without needing their own user-facing UIs.
  • Solutions Companies: Service providers that completely abstract the software layer to sell direct outcomes (e.g., 99 cents per resolved customer support ticket, automated legal filings, or real estate transactions).
  • SaaS Giants at Risk: Traditional software suites like Salesforce face severe risk. In an agentic world, system-of-record schemas can be easily generated via code, and moving data between platforms is no longer a high-friction process.

Shared Organizational Context and "Instant Apps"

  • Instant Apps: Tasklet introduced a popular generative UI feature that allows users to prompt the agent to one-shot functional web interfaces on the fly.
      • Instead of using static business intelligence dashboards, Tasklet team members use the agent to build bespoke, interactive data exploration tools in seconds.
  • The "Shared Brain" Hierarchy: To support collaborative enterprise deployments, Tasklet is rolling out a multi-tiered context system:
      • Organizational Level: High-level corporate mission, values, and branding rules.
      • Workspace/Team Level: Shared files, OKRs, and cross-agent memories. This includes centralized API integrations, allowing team members to immediately interact with corporate tools without searching for individual API keys.
      • Agent Level: Highly specific rules and assets for a single running workflow.
  • Human-in-the-Loop Governance: Rather than giving agents full autonomy over high-stakes external actions, Tasklet is optimizing push-notification approvals. For example, an agent can autonomously draft emails, but the user must click "approve" on a mobile notification before it sends.

Notable Quotes

"Really what we need is a file system that has your history, and then we need the thing that we actually send to the LLM to be just like hints at what's in the file system... Basically, you can scale from what fits in the context window, to what fits in the file system." — Andrew Lee
"A bet on us is not a bet on Anthropic, or OpenAI, or anyone else. A bet on us is a bet on everybody. We're going to give you Anthropic, OpenAI, Google, and all the open-source models, and then we will be a neutral arbiter of what you use." — Andrew Lee
"In this world, I basically think there's going to be three types of companies left in the software world: There's going to be the horizontal platforms... there'll be headless companies, and then you're going to have solutions companies where the software is totally hidden and they're selling you a product." — Andrew Lee
Hello, and welcome back to the Cognitive Revolution. Today, I'm pleased to welcome audience favorite Andrew Lee, CEO of Tasklet, back for his fourth appearance on the podcast. Andrew has always been extremely transparent and candid. His belief that speed is the only moat has made him comfortable sharing intimate details of Tasklet's agent architecture, and as you'll hear, in the six months since we last spoke, Tasklet has indeed once again entirely rewritten their stack. Today, there's much more use of file system context and agentic search to leverage available information while conserving tokens, plus a huge new emphasis on summarization at several levels of resolution. This time around, we also dig into the delicate strategic situation that Andrew and Tasklet face. While their product strategy of always betting on the models has proven correct, and Andrew's choice of Claude has been rewarded, Andrew observes that these days, everyone is fundamentally building the same thing. And today, his most intense competition is actually coming from his critical supplier, Anthropic, which, with Claude Max accounts, gives their direct customers an estimated five times as many tokens as Tasklet can purchase at the same price via the API. In micro terms, this relatively high cost of tokens has caused Tasklet to stick with Opus 4.6 rather than moving to the new 4.7. And in macro terms, it's pushing Andrew and team to become a horizontal platform capable of harnessing, or as Andrew describes it, outfitting with a mecha suit, frontier models from any provider. This evolution, which I do think Andrew has played and timed about as well as anyone possibly could, is critical because horizontal platforms are one of only three types of software companies that Andrew believes will survive the AI transition, the others being API-first companies like Stripe, and companies that develop solutions and sell outcomes, best exemplified perhaps by Finn's model of 99 cents per customer service ticket resolved. We get into lots more besides, including Tasklet's new instant apps feature, how they're thinking about deep personal and shared organizational context, the cloud container company that Andrew endorses, Tasklet's token-to-labor cost ratio, and whether or not Zuckerberg has come calling after his Mana acquisition was canceled by the Chinese government. This is a fun one with lots of valuable detail from somebody who's in the arena competing to become one of the few general-purpose AI agent platform winners and still actually willing to tell us all about it. Please enjoy my conversation with Andrew Lee, founder and CEO of Tasklet. Andrew Lee, returning champion and CEO of Tasklet, welcome back to the Cognitive Revolution. Thank you, glad to be here. You are a fan favorite and I'm going to just try to pepper you with a bunch of questions and make sure we get as much alpha for all the builders in the audience, myself included, as we can. So, first question: it's been about six months since we last spoke. You have rung in my head probably weekly with your "speed is the only moat" mantra, and I guess my first question is, what have you rebuilt in the last six months since we talked? Or maybe more to the point, what have you not rebuilt in the last six months since we talked? Yeah, I think the mantra has stayed the same, and we've rebuilt basically everything. I was, I was, I was thinking about this earlier, and even the pieces where I'm like, "Oh, this has stayed the same," actually, no, have been like totally rebuilt. So, from a product perspective, the product is actually very different now. When we launched this thing in October, it was all focused on workflow automation. We thought, "Hey, it would be really cool if, you know, people could come in, describe a workflow and we'd like run the workflow for you." But basically the feedback that we got right out of the gate was, "Hey, once I've given this agent all my context and hooked it up to all my stuff, I don't only want it just running my workflows, I also just want to be able to talk to it synchronously, too." And so, it's not just a workflow automation tool anymore. Now, it is a very general-purpose agent that is great for doing workflows, but also great for doing other types of stuff. So, that required basically a total rebuild of the product experience and, as a result, a lot of the technology behind it. So, as an example, in a workflow automation tool, in the previous iteration of the product, you basically had a main agent that you talked to for a brief period of kind of setting up your workflow. And then, once you're done setting up your workflow, you basically stop talking to that agent. So, those chats were relatively short and then, our system would kick off runs of what we call the task agent on a periodic basis when events happened. And so, every agent would have like a pretty short thing and you could do like pretty simple context engineering to make that work. In a world where you want this general-purpose agent that you can talk to synchronously and run these automations, the product experience people want is just one big linear chat, where like everything is in one chat. It works really well from a product experience, but the engineering gets very complicated because you can't just like have an infinitely long chat history that you feed into the LLM. Even if you could, it'd be really, really expensive and you wouldn't want like every automation to have to send in, you know, 10 million tokens from all the previous runs. And so, we had to kind of totally rethink the way context engineering works and say, "What if instead of the history being the thing we send to the LLM, what if the history is in the file system? What if, what if the files are the agent?" And Mark Andreessen had a thing about this, and I think people have figured this out. But, we made this switch kind of in November where we said, "Really, okay, what we need is a file system that has your history." And then, we need the thing that we actually send to the LLM to be just like hints at what's in the file system and what things you need to read to get the work done. That way, we can scale the agent up, including the number of chat messages that are sent. And there's lots of stuff we'll scale up in the future. But basically you can scale from what fits in the context window, to what fits in the file system. You can fit a lot more stuff in the file system. There's a bunch of other stuff we've rebuilt. So, another big thing we've rebuilt is around computer use. When we launched initially, computer use was like kind of this add-on. You could have a Linux machine—actually, initially it was a Windows machine, then it was a Linux machine—tacked on. And it really was kind of an afterthought. You could use it for certain things, it kind of worked okay, but most things you did with the agent didn't, didn't touch it. At this point, computer use is the absolute core of the product. So, basically everything you do is like running shell commands, touching a file system, touching a database. We have a very tightly integrated browser use experience now, where every agent has a headless VM and a browser VM that, you know, persist state across runs and allow you to do lots of really cool stuff, and it's sort of in the critical path. So, now, like if our computer use goes down, like everything goes down, versus before, it was kind of this afterthought. We've also rethought the way our integrations work. So, this is something I think from a product experience, it maybe doesn't look terribly different to folks, but the base architecture of how we plug in other systems to the Tasklet agent has been completely rethought, basically to allow the agent to have sort of more control and management of like those connections. A simple example of why, like a product experience that's improved, is it used to not be able to connect multiple instances of the same type of thing. Like you couldn't have like three different Gmail accounts connected to an agent. And now you can. So, the sort of base architecture there has been rebuilt. So, I'd say basically every line of code has probably been touched in the last six months, and like most of our fundamental assumptions have been thrown out, and the product still can do many of the same things but hopefully much better now. Yeah, that's cool. So literally, you can't think of anything that has survived the last six months? Nothing substantial. The visual design is totally different, the structure of the app is totally different, yeah, the connections system is different, the way we use computers is different, the core of the agent is different, the context management is different, the way we do compaction is different. So, it's, it's all new. Yeah, okay. Let's talk about that compaction because, I mean, one of the big takeaways from, might have been two conversations ago, was the critical importance of caching. And you had said at the time, you know, long context is, you know, it's quite effective. Obviously it's expensive. Caching, and especially the sort of 90% off caching pricing of Claude, was like critical to enabling certain things to really work in a way that wasn't, you know, tanking your, you know, what is it, the classic meme, right? "My company is dying." Making it work without killing your company. So, sounds like that's changed quite a bit. Now it's much more of like a pointers, hints type of thing. How, how is this working, and obviously tokens are like—and people are spending a lot on tokens these days, and I'm, you know, increasingly like hitting my limits on even the highest plan with Tasklet these days. So, how are you managing context for me? What should I know, what lessons should I take away from your experience on how best to manage context in the modern moment? Yeah. So, caching has actually become a much bigger deal because now that the real context is in the file system, there's just a lot more tool calls that need to be done to do the basic operations of the agent, because you're loading in a bunch of files and stuff. And so, we really have to make that caching work if we don't want this thing to be like crazy expensive. So that's, that's been very much at the forefront. We came up with a new approach to context management, that we shipped in December, that basically works like this. You take your whole chat history, and you put it in the file system. So, it's all accessible in the file system. And then, you find a way to summarize that whole history in kind of a fixed-length number of tokens by having recent stuff be included in like sort of high granularity—like the last thing you said, you'll probably have most of it or all of it there. And then, older things basically have like decreasing fidelity as you go back. So, if you have a very long chat, the stuff in your current turn, like the current thing that's running, it's probably all going to be there, including like all the thinking blocks, and all the tool call responses, and, and, you know, all the files and things are probably going to be sent to the LLM verbatim, depending on how long the run is. But for most kind of short runs, that'll be the case. The previous turn will probably mostly be there. You're going to have the full user message, you'll probably have the assistant response, you'll probably have the tool call arguments, you'll probably have the tool call responses, and you'll probably have the thinking blocks. But as you go farther back, we start stripping the thinking blocks. We start stripping the tool call responses, or at least truncating the tool call responses and then stripping them. We start truncating and then stripping the tool call arguments. Then we start collapsing tool calls. And then we start, you know, shrinking down the assistant messages. And then, finally, we get to some LLM-based summarization, and we do this in buckets moving back, so that we can have sort of a minimal impact on caching. Basically, you want to like avoid messing with prefixes as much as you can, so as you go back, basically you get into these buckets where we have like different levels of compression. And those buckets, as they get older, tend to like get added to very slowly, and then, once they hit a certain threshold, like we shrink them down. And this system has actually worked basically like pretty well. And the core thesis basically is like, you generally care a lot more about recent stuff. And you trust the agent to go and like look things up when it needs to. And I would say, it's not perfect. We do definitely have people say that agents forget things. It definitely does still cost us a lot of money to run. But I think it's generally worked. And our plan is to double down on this type of architecture. We have lots of ideas of how to improve this, but the basic approach of these sort of like decreasing fidelity as you go back in these like bucketed, cache-aware chunks, I think is the right approach. So, how often does that get updated? Like, if I have an agent that runs on a daily basis, do you try to keep the cache active from one day to the next? Or is it sort of every day we're going to have a fresh cache that will run through that whole session and all the interactions, but kind of tomorrow, you know, do we begin again? Or do we, like, on what frequency do we begin again? Well, there's, there's two pieces. There is, one, the things that get updated on our side—like when do we decide what that compressed history that we put in the LLM looks like? And then, what caching do we do on the LLM side? And the answer to the former is, every time you do anything, it's sort of incrementally updated, including in the middle of runs. If you have like a very long turn that has a lot of tokens, it might actually start compressing inside that turn. And the reason that we persist that is actually calculating it can be really expensive. Like running an LLM-based compaction of like an older section eats up a lot of tokens, and you don't have to do that every time the thing starts up if, you know, every hour you have a trigger running, and every time you have to like compress a bunch of history, that can be very expensive. So, we keep all that around. On the model side, caching depends on the provider. So, in the case of Anthropic, we're using five-minute caching, and so, it doesn't stick around very long. And the assumption there basically is, you're probably either in an active session, or like in the middle of a turn, in which case five-minute caching is enough. Or, you're probably waiting for the next trigger to run, and like most people's triggers are not running like every, you know, half hour, they're running every few hours or every day. So, the assumption there is this is not so common. And then different providers have different possibilities there, and for example, OpenAI has much nicer caching primitives, for example, and I'm happy to, happy to talk about those, too. Yeah, okay. That's interesting. So, it's basically constant maintenance of the higher-level summaries that will be fed into the LLM, and then pretty short, kind of single-burst style caching to actually reduce the cost of incremental calls within like one agent run. And it sounds like at least for Anthropic, that kind of is typically limited to like, the cache is hit for the, for one run, but not hit across runs for the most part. Yeah, that's, that's the current approach. Oh, one thing I want to note is the way our system is built today, we basically get no cache benefit across users. So, it's like caching for, actually even, even per agent, like it's basically caching per agent. There's some changes that we can make to do a lot of cache optimization across agents, and potentially even across organizations and users. And, I don't want to get into the specifics of that, because that's, that's still, that's still upcoming, but I think there's a lot of potential actually to save money, across agents as well. Yeah, okay. Well, that'll be important. Well, let's, I do want to circle back to the OpenAI question, because you guys have been Claude maximalists, and your other mantra that rings in my head a lot is always bet on the models. I'd say it's safe to say that that bet has gone well over the last six months. Obviously, we've seen some of the most notable model releases in the sense that the community has sort of flagged, like, 3.5 and 3.6 as kind of qualitative shifts where, like, things went from not working to working, and people are like, "Oh, I can really get pretty general-purpose knowledge work out of these things on a pretty consistent basis now." I would love to hear how you would characterize the advances that we've seen. Maybe you could do that in terms of like what new use cases have opened up, maybe, you know, things that have surprised you, possibly also like things that are still not working, that, you know, might be surprising given all the things that do work. And then, we can get to the latest models. I want to, so, kind of give me the like 3.5, 3.6 history, and then we can go to 3.7 and 3.5 present. Yeah, I think the overall, you know, approach of always bet on the models, I think that has—I totally agree—that has like totally held up. When we started working on Tasklet, we were using Claude 3.4, and that actually could get you a long ways and it worked pretty well. 3.5 was a big unlock. So, 3.5 started to work much better at doing computer use, and it could just like manage sort of navigating through the various different connections and tools enablement process and stuff much better. And that, you know, came out very early in the lifetime of Tasklet and was like, I think, a big bonus for us. And the cost reductions around Opus were huge for us, in, I want to say, December when that happened, they dropped. So, initially, like we could really only have people on Sonnet, and then, Opus dropped from 15 to 5, and that was like a big unlock for us as well. I think 3.6 was like a solid incremental change, made it nicer for, again, for computer use, which has become increasingly important for us, both like headless and headful, as well as code gen, and that enabled our instant apps feature, which, I think we'll probably talk about today, which is a very cool feature. 3.7, we actually haven't rolled out yet. I think 3.7 has been much better in certain areas. Like, it's much better to kind of like, you know, code, and like one-shotting kind of long projects. But for the types of like iterative knowledge work that we support, it doesn't actually seem like a huge boost, and it's a lot more expensive. So, the tokenizer changes they made increased our cost by like 30%, and costs are huge for us because we, you know, we essentially pass them on to users. And so, we actually opted not to ship 3.7 as like a default recommended model. We are going to ship it, but as sort of an advanced user option if people want to, and we're going to like note that, "Hey, this actually like costs a lot more." So, I think, I think the progress there has been great. The reason we started on Anthropic, and have been so Anthropic focused for so long, was just that the basic core of our agent—like the ability for it to like navigate through a discovery process of connections and activate the right tool in your agent, and then manage its context, the way it manages context, just the core inner workings—requires kind of a base level of intelligence that the other models just couldn't do. They couldn't, you basically couldn't use the same harness with them and have it expect it to work well. That has changed, which is really exciting for us. It's, you know, it's kind of scary to be like we're totally dependent on this one vendor, even though they're great. Like, Anthropic models are amazing. Don't get me wrong. But, supply chain risks are everywhere if you take that approach. Exactly. But more recently, GPT-3.5 has gotten really good. I think it's a step up for our usecase over 3.4. We will, by the time this podcast comes out, we will probably have announced this publicly. So just, hey, it's, it's going to be there very soon. And it can navigate our harness super well. I think it gives Opus 3.6 a run for its money for most usecases. So, that's, I think that's really exciting. And I, and I'm pretty optimistic about the OpenAI roadmap this year. I think they made a huge bet on compute last year, and I think that's starting to show. And I think they're where they're going to have a lead for a while. And it also is clear that they've focused their business much more on these types of usecases. And you saw like the progress Codex made over like six months, and if they put that level of effort into tuning the models around these types of agentic usecases, I think that'll be, that'll be huge. So, we, you know, we signed a, we signed a deal with OpenAI the other day, and, we'll, we'll be launching stuff there, and we're making a, making a pretty big bet there as well. There's also, there's progress in other places, though. The latest Google models are pretty solid. They're not, they're not at the level of Anthropic, OpenAI yet, in my opinion, but they are making very fast progress and they're much closer. And then, we've been playing with, with DeepSeek and with Kimi. And, like, the latest Kimi is, you know, as far as we can tell, like maybe better than Haiku and cheaper. So, I think we're going to see those probably make their way into Tasklet soon. So, I would expect, you know, within the next few months, we are going to have, you know, Anthropic models, OpenAI models, open-source models, Google models. I bet you the Anthropic ones will still be the, you know, the best and probably recommended in most cases, but people will have a variety of choices and like some good cost, cost-optimization options for certain things. So many, just, followups there. Let's maybe start with what I imagine has been a little bit of a delicate dance with Anthropic, and then we can kind of compare and contrast that with what OpenAI is now bringing to the table. I don't know, you probably know, what the ratio is of API cost to effective token cost when you buy a Claude Max subscription and max it out. And, obviously, in the, you know, intervening time since we spoke last, there's been the whole OpenClaude phenomenon, and that's its own, you know, bunch of drama with you can, you can't, you sort of can but you got to pay the API price, we're lowering our limits, we're, you know, buying compute from XAI, we're raising our limits back again. What, what has been like from your perspective to be building on a platform that you're also sort of competing with, that is kind of undercutting you to various levels at different times on their pricing? Yeah, it, it, it's definitely a, it's definitely an interesting relationship. So, like on the one hand, the models are amazing, right? They're super good for our usecase and like, their team has been like really helpful and responsive, and like, we talk to them on a very regular basis, and they are, you know, they're trying hard to support us and we get early access to stuff and they take our feedback and all that. So like, they're a great partner, I don't want anyone to think I, I, I think otherwise. But at the same time, if you look at our stats of like when someone churns off Tasklet, where do they go? Like, 80% of those users go to an Anthropic product. So, they are like a very direct competitor. And, I, you know, I think there's different use cases where like we shine, or their product shine, but clear that they're a very direct competitor. And the number one reason that they do that is because they already have a Max plan, and they don't want to have to spend additional money on, on Tasklet. And so, basically, you know, every time they release a new model update, we're like, "This is great, this is awesome, right?" And every time they, they send an email being like, "Now your Max plan is even cooler," we're like, "Crap, right? This is just going to make it harder." And so, they definitely, they definitely subsidized it, and definitely have set some distorted expectations with folks around like what you can get for a certain price, and so, cost is like a constant, constant struggle for us to try to, you know, help users use the products more efficiently, and help them understand, "Hey, we're, we're actually working at like some pretty razor-thin margins here, and trying to make this cheap for you guys." So, okay, one more thing on the Anthropic dance, and then obviously this gives you, you know, a lot of incentive to broaden out and try to position yourself a little bit differently. I mean, they're, you know, increasingly we're sort of seeing like the model is being trained in the first-party harness. Like, I guess, for one thing, how is that even possible? And how do you think about trying to compete with what they themselves are going to build given, you know, all the inside knowledge and advanced prep and, you know, kind of close coupling advantages that they have? At a high level, I kind of think that everyone is building the same thing. Like, like, you have all these different agent companies and basically over time, as the models get smarter, and the agents build in more sort of general-purpose tools, you know, computer use, and file systems, and whatnot, you can do very similar things in many things. So, you can go into Claude Code, and you can do all kinds of non-coding things in Claude Code. You can, and, you know, CodeX, and Claude Code, and like many of these other products are all, you know, able to do coding and non-coding things like pretty well. I think where you start to differentiate, and I do think you can differentiate within this space to some extent, is really around like what you're optimizing for, and what the ergonomics are. So, in our case, take Tasklet. You can totally like write code with Tasklet, you can hook it up to your GitHub, and you can have it like generate PRs, and it does it just fine. And like, we do this for marketing, for example, if we put on a new blog post or whatever, like, I, I, I write the content in Tasklet, and then I just have it generate some PR, and it works fine. But, it's not going to be as smart, and definitely not as cost-effective, for like heavy-duty coding, as going and using like an actual coding harness. And, it's definitely not going to be as nice to use, because the actual coding harness is like going to be in like, you know, Conductor or something that's like designed for like a coding workflow, and our product is like not set up that way. So, I, I see a future where you can kind of pick up any AI agent, and kind of do anything. But, different ones are going to have sort of different like cost and performance trade-offs, and different ones are going to have like just different ergonomics for like the different types of work. Where we really shine, is 24/7 automation of knowledge work for companies. Especially knowledge work for companies that is not like your personal work, but like something the company owns. So, if you have, you know, simple example, you have some like complex invoicing process at your company, you don't want to be running that in like your local cowork, right? Like, if, if you close your laptop, and like the company can't invoice people anymore, that's bad. And, you know, you don't want to like put that in OpenClaude, and put it on your Mac Mini in the corner, because again, if someone like trips over the power cord, like, well, you can't run your invoicing. What you really want, is something that's running in the cloud, and is manageable by many people, and you have a lot of infrastructure around it to like manage and provide oversight, and like, you know, have audit logs, and have guardrails around the thing, and you can control costs on your different agents, and so, there's a lot of like kind of team enablement features you care a lot about. And, that's where we really shine, and a lot of the, the work to make that work well, is actually kind of fundamental to the way the agent is built. So, I talked about our context manager, the reason our context manager is built the way it is, is because we want to have triggers as sort of regular messages into the agent, which means these agents, if you have an agent that's like running a trigger every time you get an email, that agent might fire, you know, 10,000 times this year. And so, you need an agent that can fire 10,000 times and like still remember things at the beginning of the chat, and still behave in a reasonable way, and that's a pretty different thing to optimize for than like a coding session, like the types of, you know, the way Claude Code sort of like resets context, like makes total sense in a coding environment, doesn't really make sense in, in a world where it's like processing all your email. So, that's how I see us differentiating. The other, the other note, couple of notes I want to make here on differentiation, is, one, the market is just freaking huge. So, if you look at coding agents, and you might say like, 'Oh, clearly Claude Code and CodeX have won.' So, you know, if we end up, I'd love to be the, the number one here, but if we end up being like number four or five or six, like it could still be a very, very significant exit. And then the last thing I want to note, is, and I think this is probably the most important point, when we go and pitch a business, what we're, what we're trying to help them do, is deploy AI for real inside their company to like automate stuff. And, those, like typical company is, they don't want to have to spend all their time researching AI models and like placing bets on which lab is going to win, right? They, they want to choose a platform that's going to serve them well, and they want to benefit from everybody's improvements over time. And so, we can go in there and say, "Hey, a bet on us is not a bet on Anthropic, or OpenAI, or anyone else, a bet on us is a bet on everybody. We're going to give you, you know, Anthropic model, OpenAI models, Google's, and all the, Google, and all the open-source models, and then we will be a neutral like arbiter of what you use. And so, if, you know, to the extent that we can build features to help you choose the right model for the job, and like optimize your costs across the different things, like you can trust us because none of these are ours, right? Like we're getting the same margins on everything, and so, you know, we're a neutral party versus if you go with Anthropic, you know, right now, it's just Anthropic products." "Even if they decided to like, you know, provide other models through their product, which they could, although I don't think they're going to, but they could, I don't know if you'd really trust them to do that in a neutral way. So, a year ago, right before we, we started the pivot, one of the, like the, the big thing that we were seeing was—and for context, for, for people who, who maybe don't know, we had a product called Shortwave, which was an AI email client. We still, we still have it actually, but it's not the focus of the company anymore. And we had this really nice embedded agent inside, and you could do like really cool email stuff. And we realized that it wasn't going to be too long before you could take a product like, you know, ChatGPT, and you could say, "Show me my inbox," and it would just like generate a UI for your email on the spot. And once that worked well, you wouldn't need an AI email client, right? Because the whole email part would go away, our entire concept of differentiation where we're like, "Hey, we're going to embed this agent inside a, like a custom-built UI," that had a shelf life. And, you know, the product is actually still growing, it's still, it's still doing really reasonably well, but like in 10 years, I don't think it's going to be around, probably, probably much less than 10 years, I don't think it's going to be around in, at least not in this form. So, we said, "Man, we, we can't build a business around an AI agent embedded in a UI, we need to, we need to do something else." And so, we said, "Hey, we're going to build like a very general-purpose agent that isn't relying on this, and we're going to go after doing an agent for a specific type of workflow," or like these sort of like knowledge work, trigger-based knowledge work workflows. So, then we built the thing we launched in October, and the feedback from people was like, "Hey, we don't want to have like one tool for workflow automation and another tool for doing our day-to-day work because we want them all to have the same context. So, I don't want to have to like maintain two systems where they both have all the, all the stuff in the shared brain, I just want to have one system." And so, we said, "Okay, I guess we need to do not just the workflow stuff, but we need to do the, the synchronous stuff as well." And, again, you know, when, when we pivoted out of email, it was like, "Okay, well actually there's going to be some like more general product that's going to encompass this stuff." And then, again, it was like, "Oh, I guess it's going to be some more general product, product that's going to encompass this stuff." And we, in March, we launched our Instant Apps feature, which is basically a generative UI feature. So, the, the idea is what if you could generate any UI you want that hooks up to any of the data in any of your connections and this works instantly in a single prompt? And, it's clear that like each area where we, like we feel like there's going to be differentiation is like falling away. And, we, we basically need to assume that the number of, the number of these products that win is going to be relatively small. Like, I don't think we're going to have many, many, many, many tools that all have a, you know, AI embedded in them. So, rather than, you know, today, the way most knowledge workers work is like they're switching between tabs, or they're switching between apps on, in their dock, and they're like, "You know, sometimes they're using, they're using, you know, Word, and then sometimes they're using Notion, and then sometimes they're using Linear, and then sometimes they're using Gmail, and they're going from tab to tab to tab for different things." And, we think that entire world is going away. Instead, you're going to have one app that has a UI, it's going to be your AI agent, hopefully Tasklet. If you want to do some, you know, if you want to access some data from one of these tools, you connect to it through API. If you want to do some interesting analysis, that analysis rather than being done by some like bespoke, you know, business logic in the tool, it's done via code gen. So, the agent generates the code and like runs the analysis. If you want a UI, the agent generates the UI one-shotted with the prompt and gives you the UI you need, and we think it can cover basically all of your productivity software. And in this world, I basically think there's going to be three types of companies left in, in the software world: There's going to be the horizontal platforms, of which I think there'll be a very few numbers of, very few winners because people don't want to have to, have to maintain context and connections across a bunch of platforms. So, let me give you a few examples of how I think we can help. So, one, as you mentioned, like, like versioning. There's a whole bunch of startups working on file systems for agents right now, and some of those folks are working on versioning. The basic idea is like, "Hey, if your agent goes rogue, you just want to like roll back to some previous state." And like, in a simple chatbot, you can just like throw away messages at the end. But, in something that's like touching the world, you've got to be able to roll back the world, and so, there's a lot of... you know, for a file system, you can just change the file system, but if it's touched APIs and stuff, you might need to like keep logs of things. But, the ability for you to like undo things the agent does I think is, is pretty key, so I think there's a lot you can do there. I think another area is having like oversight and like, and like logging and stuff so you actually have the ability to like have a human in the loop in places where it matters, and do that in smart ways. And so, like with our product today, like, you have to activate tools. One of the things we're going to be adding soon is the ability for you to like have some tools that you approve every run. In our case, email is a perfect example of this where like, people are pretty confident to say, "Hey, you can read my emails as much as you want. You can make as many drafts as you want, but you can't send anything unless I say yes." And, we want to get to the point where, you know, that is really ergonomic. So, for example, like, it can send you a push notification when it's ready to send an email where like, it'll go crazy reading and searching and making drafts and then when it's ready to send, you get a push notification, it's like, "Hey, do you want to review this before it goes?" and then you can say yes, and that's all like pushed to you. So, I think permission's going to be another big area. I think another big area is using code better and in, in, and in a more like mechanical way. So, you know, let's, let's take data migration from like one system to another. The naive way to do this is to like load that data through an API, feed it into the LLM, have the LLM then like, like call some tools to like put it somewhere else. And basically, when you do that, every time you're sort of like putting it through language model context and trusting it to like not hallucinate and like reproduce that data, which like, I think the models get better at over time, but like, it's very hard to have a lot of confidence there. The better way to do this is have the model just like generate a migration script, and then run the migration script, and that gives you like an artifact in the middle that you can test, and you can have human approval for. So like, I think, yeah, if you're, if you're moving data from one to the other, you still want to have an agent that's like thinking through how to solve the problem, but what it should probably do is like generate a migration script, generate some tests, run the tests, and then send the thing to the human being like, "Here we have the migration plan and the code, and the test, and this is why we think it's going to work, are you okay with this?" and then you say yes, and then we run it. You can even have test environments, right? So, I think the ability to have like tools within the agent that allow it to do like really high-level stuff and to have approval, there's a lot of opportunity there. Okay, I know time is short. BREAKING: Trump addresses Xi's WARNING over Taiwan

Fox News(15.3M)·24:21·1.1M views·2026-05-15
Donald TrumpPresident of the United States Xi JinpingPresident of China
TL;DRPresident Trump’s Beijing summit focused on leveraging personal rapport with President Xi to secure massive trade deals while maintaining a "status quo" policy on Taiwan and prioritizing the total neutralization of Iran’s nuclear capabilities over short-term domestic economic pressures.

TL;DR

President Trump’s Beijing summit focused on leveraging personal rapport with President Xi to secure massive trade deals while maintaining a "status quo" policy on Taiwan and prioritizing the total neutralization of Iran’s nuclear capabilities over short-term domestic economic pressures.

Trade and Domestic Manufacturing

  • Boeing and GE Agreements: Trump announced a commitment from China to purchase 200 Boeing aircraft (777s and 737s), with the potential to scale up to 750 units if performance milestones are met. These planes will utilize General Electric engines.
  • Tariff Strategy: The administration is maintaining a 100% tariff on Chinese electric vehicles to prevent the "destruction" of the U.S. auto industry, citing the current economic struggles of European manufacturers as a cautionary tale.
  • Reshoring Incentives: To avoid tariffs, Trump is encouraging Chinese firms to build manufacturing plants on U.S. soil and hire American workers. He noted that the U.S. has seen $18 trillion in investment over the last 11 months.
  • Regulatory Workarounds: Following a specific Supreme Court ruling against certain tariffs, Trump stated he is pursuing a "more stringent" and "complex" alternative legal route to maintain trade pressure.

Taiwan and Semiconductor Security

  • The "Independence" Red Line: Trump acknowledged President Xi’s stance that Taiwan is the most critical issue in bilateral relations. Trump’s stated preference is to maintain the status quo, warning that a move toward Taiwanese independence would be "risky" and met with "harsh" consequences from China.
  • Semiconductor Repatriation: Trump accused Taiwan of having "stolen" the U.S. chip industry in previous decades.
      • Relocation Goal: He is pushing for Taiwanese chip manufacturers to move operations to Arizona and other U.S. locations.
      • Market Share Target: The administration expects the U.S. to control 40–50% of the global chip business by the end of his term.
  • Military Aid as Leverage: The President confirmed he is holding $12 billion in approved weapons for Taiwan "in abeyance," using the package as a negotiating chip with China.

Global Security and the Iran Conflict

  • Military Status: Trump claimed the U.S. has effectively destroyed the Iranian Navy and Air Force, stating that their ships are currently "on the bottom of the sea." He compared current military efficiency to the recent intervention in Venezuela, which he claimed took only "48 minutes."
  • Nuclear Non-Proliferation: The primary strategic goal is the total prevention of an Iranian nuclear weapon. Trump dismissed concerns over Iranian threats to the Strait of Hormuz, asserting that China (which receives 40% of its oil from the region) also wants the conflict to end.
  • Intelligence Capabilities: Trump highlighted the capabilities of the "Space Force," noting that U.S. satellite technology can read individual nametags on personnel at Iranian nuclear sites from space.

Technology and the AI Race

  • U.S. Leadership: Trump asserted that the U.S. has overtaken China’s early lead in Artificial Intelligence. He attributed this to new policies allowing AI companies to build their own private electric-producing plants to bypass grid limitations.
  • Guardrails and Competition: While the President discussed AI "guardrails" with President Xi, he expressed skepticism about their efficacy given the intense nature of the bilateral competition.
  • Medical Breakthroughs: He cited AI-driven advancements in medicine and disease cures as the primary positive output of the technology.

Domestic Economy and Energy Prices

  • Short-Term Pain vs. Long-Term Security: Acknowledging "painfully high" gas prices and inflation, Trump argued that preventing a nuclear-armed Iran is more important than short-term financial fluctuations.
  • Market Performance: Trump cited record highs in the Dow (50,000) and S&P 7,000, along with all-time high 401(k) balances, as evidence that the underlying economy is strong enough to weather the current geopolitical friction.
  • Price Outlook: He predicted that energy prices will "drop like a rock" once the Iranian situation is resolved and "loaded" oil tankers are released to the market.

Trump-Xi Summit, Benioff: "Not My First SaaSpocalypse," OpenAI vs Apple, Multi-Sensory AI, El Niño

All-In Podcast(1.1M)·76:31·208.2K views·2026-05-15
Marc BenioffCEO, Salesforce Donald TrumpUS President Xi JinpingPresident of China
TL;DR"I’m not a Democrat or a Republican.
Salesforce CEO Marc Benioff and the "Besties" analyze the Trump-Xi summit's focus on economic entanglement as a deterrent to war, while addressing the "SaaSpocalypse" market rerating and the shift toward multi-sensory, agentic AI models. Strategic Entanglement: The...

TL;DR: Salesforce CEO Marc Benioff and the "Besties" analyze the Trump-Xi summit's focus on economic entanglement as a deterrent to war, while addressing the "SaaSpocalypse" market rerating and the shift toward multi-sensory, agentic AI models.

U.S.-China Relations: Peace Through Trade

  • Strategic Entanglement: The Trump-Xi summit focused on "economic entanglement" as a primary tool for peace.
      • Trade Deals: China committed to purchasing 200 Boeing jets, U.S. oil, LNG, and massive quantities of soybeans via Cargill.
      • The "Board of Trade" Philosophy: Benioff emphasized that business is the "greatest platform for change," suggesting that deeper financial ties reduce the likelihood of conflict.
  • The Taiwan Issue: The panel debated the future of Taiwan as a geopolitical flashpoint.
      • Diminishing Strategic Value: David Friedberg argued that as both the U.S. (via TSMC in Arizona) and China mainland their chip fabrication, the strategic necessity of Taiwan may decrease by 2040.
      • Chip Sales: Chamath Palihapitiya advocated for continuing to sell high-end chips to China to ensure U.S. companies like Nvidia maintain market dominance over domestic Chinese competitors like Huawei.
  • Corporate Diplomacy: Prominent CEOs (Elon Musk, Tim Cook, Jensen Huang) are serving as unofficial ambassadors, leveraging their unique access to President Xi to maintain business stability.

The "SaaSpocalypse" and Software's AI Pivot

  • Market Rerating: Enterprise software stocks (Salesforce, ServiceNow, Workday) have faced 37-45% declines.
      • Valuation Shift: Benioff noted that companies like HubSpot are trading at unprecedented lows (2x sales), describing this as a "hypnosis around AI" where the market is waiting for productivity gains to manifest in financial results.
      • Internal Efficiency: Salesforce is spending $300M on Anthropic tokens to automate coding and support, significantly reducing development costs.
  • The Rise of Agents: The focus of SaaS is shifting from human-operated software to "Agentforce"—autonomous agents that handle customer interactions and BDR (Business Development Representative) functions.
      • The Semantic Layer: Benioff argued that AI is "probabilistic" and requires a "source of truth," which Salesforce provides through integrated data (evidenced by the $8B-$9B Informatica acquisition).
  • SaaS Survival: Chamath noted that the "low end of the market is finished," but high-end monoliths with deep C-suite relationships remain secure as they become the primary distributors of AI tokens to enterprises.

AI Infrastructure and the OpenAI-Apple Rift

  • Strategic Friction: OpenAI is reportedly considering legal action against Apple due to the failed integration of ChatGPT into Siri.
      • Gripes: OpenAI claims Apple has not promoted the integration and requires users to explicitly name "ChatGPT," limiting subscription conversions.
      • Competition: Tensions are high as OpenAI recruits Apple talent (like Jony Ive) to build competing hardware.
  • Local vs. Cloud AI: Jason Calacanis argued that Apple’s hardware (M5 chips with high RAM) creates a path for local, private models, while Chamath believes "persistence" across devices will keep the advantage with cloud-based models.
  • Multi-Sensory Models: The panel highlighted the transition from text-based LLMs to real-time, multi-sensory models that "watch" desktops and listen to audio every 200 milliseconds, which could increase token consumption by 1,000x.

Global Risks: El Niño and Secondary Markets

  • Climate Crisis (Science Corner): Friedberg warned of a historically strong El Niño in 2026, driven by 11 million terawatt hours of excess energy stored in the oceans.
      • Food Security: High probability of monsoon failure in India and crop failure in Brazil, potentially leading to a global "calorie deficit" and civil unrest in South Asia.
      • Economic Impact: Expect spiking energy prices and grid failures in the U.S. Southwest and Canada.
  • Anthropic’s Secondary Market Crackdown: Anthropic is moving to negate "Dark SPVs" (Special Purpose Vehicles) that charge predatory fees (10% load-in fees) to retail investors.
      • The "Bullet" to SPVs: Chamath praised the move, arguing that multi-layered SPVs are "disgraciad" and that companies should go public sooner to rationalize their equity structures.

Notable Quotes

"I’m not a Democrat or a Republican. I’m an American... I believe business is the greatest platform for change." — Marc Benioff
"The simplest and surest path to a no-conflict détente with China is economic entanglement. It has to be bidirectional." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"We are at the end of the 'hands-on-keyboard' era. Most of our engineers just speak... it's all talk, pedal, and Whisper Flow." — Marc Benioff
"This El Niño event goes beyond an interesting weather anecdote. We are looking at temperatures four degrees above normal—the oceans are the battery of weather, and that energy is about to be released." — David Friedberg
