Building AlphaGo from scratch – Eric Jang

Dwarkesh Patel(1.3M)·157:18·18.7K views·2026-05-15
Dwarkesh PatelHost, Dwarkesh Patel Podcast Eric JangAI Researcher
TL;DRRebuilding AlphaGo with modern AI tools demonstrates how Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) provides a mathematically superior training target that sidesteps the credit assignment problem plaguing modern LLM reinforcement learning, while highlighting…
board games as an ideal,...

TL;DR

Rebuilding AlphaGo with modern AI tools demonstrates how Monte Carlo Tree Search (MCTS) provides a mathematically superior training target that sidesteps the credit assignment problem plaguing modern LLM reinforcement learning, while highlighting board games as an ideal, non-cheatable sandbox for benchmarking automated AI research agents.

The Architectural Primitives of AlphaGo and Modern Go Bots

  • Trump-Taylor Rules: Unlike traditional Chinese or Japanese scoring rules which contain subjective human ambiguities regarding "dead" stones, the mathematically unambiguous Trump-Taylor rules are used to train computer Go systems because they can be resolved perfectly and deterministically by a computer.
  • The PUCT Action Selection Formula: AlphaGo utilizes PUCT (Predictor Upper Confidence with Trees) to navigate the game's massive branching factor (historically starting at 361 moves per turn).
      • Importance Sampling: PUCT scales its exploration bonus using the neural network's policy prediction ($P$). This trick prunes the search space from 361 moves down to the 3 or 4 most promising actions, making tree search highly tractable.
  • ResNets vs. Transformers: In low-compute, small-data regimes, Residual Networks (ResNets) still outperform Transformers.
      • Convolutional Inductive Bias: ResNets naturally benefit from spatial locality (convolutions), which matches local Go board battles.
      • Global Feature Pooling: While vision transformers natively capture global context, modern Go engines like *KataGo* achieve similar results by manually injecting globally aggregated features into convolutional layers, bypassing the massive data requirements needed to train global attention from scratch.
  • Massive Compute Reductions: In 2020, David Wu of Jane Street released *KataGo*, which achieved a 40x reduction in the compute required to train a superhuman Go bot tabula rasa.
      • Due to faster GPU hardware (such as NVIDIA Blackwell and Ada Lovelace architectures) and improved libraries, a superhuman 9x9 Go bot can now be trained on a single consumer GPU in under 24 hours for less than $100.

The Policy Iteration Loop and Search Distillation

  • The Core Self-Play Loop: AlphaGo Zero's learning engine relies on a four-step iterative process executed during every move:

1. Selection: Walk down the existing MCTS tree from the root, selecting actions that maximize the PUCT formula.

2. Expansion: Once a leaf node is reached, dynamic game engine rules are evaluated to append its children.

3. Evaluation: The neural network evaluates the leaf node, outputting a win probability ($V$) and raw policy probabilities ($P$). AlphaGo Zero completely discarded computationally expensive random "rollouts" to the end of the game, relying solely on this single network evaluation.

4. Backup: Walk back up the path, incrementing visit counts ($N$) and updating the mean action values ($Q$) of parent nodes.

  • Distillation as the Improvement Operator: During self-play, MCTS acts as a policy improvement operator. The final search yields a highly optimized visit distribution ($\Pi$). The raw policy network ($P$) is then trained to directly predict $\Pi$.
      • This distillation forces the raw network's instant, "shoot-from-the-hip" intuition to match the performance of a computationally intensive 1,000-step lookahead search.
  • Value Function Grounding: MCTS can collapse if the value network's predictions are inaccurate. To ground the model early in training, developers should warm-start the network using expert human datasets (like AlphaGo Lee) or co-train on smaller 9x9 boards to build robust endgame intuitions before transitioning to 19x19 boards.

Why AlphaGo’s RL is Fundamentally Superior to LLM RL

  • The Credit Assignment Problem: Modern LLM alignment (via RLHF or policy gradient methods like REINFORCE) struggles with credit assignment. If a model generates a 100k+ token trajectory and receives a correct answer at the end, the algorithm must naively guess which of those 100k tokens actually drove the success—a dynamic described as "sucking supervision through a straw."
  • Local step-by-step Supervision: MCTS sidesteps this entirely by providing a highly informative, low-variance training target at *every single step* of the trajectory.
      • Rather than up-weighting all tokens in a winning game and down-weighting those in a losing game, the model trains on the soft probability distribution ($\Pi$) generated by a local planner.
  • The Cold Start Trap: In a naive RL setting with a large action space (such as an LLM vocabulary of 100k+ tokens), an untrained model has a near-0% chance of randomly stumbling upon a winning multi-step trajectory.
      • Log-Scale Pass Rates: Supervised learning provides massive information gains in this low-pass-rate regime, whereas naive RL learns almost nothing because its signal is bounded by the flat entropy of binary coin flips.
      • MCTS circumvents this by using local, step-by-step search to iteratively climb out of the 0% success rate trap, even when the model cannot yet visualize a complete winning path.

The Future of Automated AI Research and Self-Improvement Loops

  • Squeezing Performance via Automation: Eric Jang successfully used Claude (referred to as Opus 4.6/4.7 in the transcript) as a highly capable, grad-student-level assistant.
      • Open-Ended Optimization: The model successfully executed complex, end-to-end experiment pipelines, including writing PyTorch code, diagnosing gradient bottlenecks, managing data-loader augmentations, and analyzing training anomalies.
      • Interactive Plotting: By configuring a custom "experiment" skill, Jang could instruct the model to run experiments, generate custom charts, compile comprehensive reports, and propose structural hypotheses.
  • The Lateral Thinking Bottleneck: While current frontier models are exceptional at local hyperparameter optimization, they fail at macro-level research direction. Current systems struggle to perform lateral thinking—such as stepping back from a failing research track, identifying a fundamental assumption error, and returning to first principles to rewrite the core infrastructure.
  • Go as an Outer Loop for Self-Improvement: To train automated AI research agents, the AI community needs fast, cheap, and uncheatable outer verification loops.
      • A Standardized Sandbox: Go serves as a perfect environment. An agent's research modifications (such as testing a new auxiliary loss or architecture) can be verified objectively via its win rate against *KataGo*.
      • This provides a reliable, scalable training ground for automated scientists before deploying them to noisier, more expensive real-world tasks like drug discovery or robotics.

Notable Quotes

"It’s extremely beautiful. It’s like, you have a bootstrapping process where you start with a weak policy, you use search to make it stronger, you train a neural net to predict that stronger policy, and then you repeat." — *Eric Jang, former VP of AI at 1X / Senior Research Scientist at Google DeepMind*
"The problem with Go and chess is that the other player is always trying to do some shit... So things can kind of drift off, and you always want to be able to correct back to your winning condition." — *Eric Jang, former VP of AI at 1X / Senior Research Scientist at Google DeepMind*
"Monte Carlo tree search is doing something very fundamentally different, which is it’s not trying to do credit assignment on wins; it’s trying to improve the label for any given action you took. And so, the variance of your learning signal is very low compared to the alternative naive RL thing." — *Eric Jang, former VP of AI at 1X / Senior Research Scientist at Google DeepMind*

And, in perfect information games, there does exist a Nash equilibrium strategy for which you can do no worse than any other strategy. So, if you know that your opponent has a particular bias, like, they, they love to play aggressively, you can actually in principle counter that specific strategy better than a Nash equilibrium policy. But, to counter any given strategy, there does exist a single, Nash equilibrium that can be decided solely using the current state. Mhm. So, that is a design choice that most Go agents, AlphaGo chose to do, which in hindsight turned out to work very well because the, Nash equilibrium seems to be superhuman, like, like, like no human strategy seems to be able to beat it. So, we have step one, which is selection. So, in selection, we start from the root node. Let's just say we've already run 100 simulations, so we have some sort of tree that has already been built, and UCB, or PUCT has been populated, so we have visit counts on everything, we have Q, we have P, we have all of that. So, starting from S equals S root, and while S, is not, a leaf of the tree, we are going to, select, A, that maximizes, this PUCT quantity. Now, we haven't brought in, you know, the neural network's policy, P, yet, so we should explain, like, where does P come in. The original AlphaGo paper, they had, as you mentioned, this supervised learning policy network, and so they used, we're going to call that like, P, SL, of action given S. Mhm. But, in AlphaGo Zero and also KataGo, you basically have, just like a single policy network, so we'll just write P, S, A. So, this is the neural network's policy prediction. It's a, it's a vector of logits, softmaxed over 361 moves. Mhm. And, and we basically just plug that in right here. Now, we talked about before, like, where did P of A come from? And, in, in UCB, there was no P of A. But, in PUCT, you can think of it like importance sampling, right? The neural network already has a really good guess of what actions are good, so we're going to scale our exploration bonus by the, probability that the neural network thinks that this is a good action. And, and so if we have, if we've never visited this node before, we're still going to favor nodes for which the neural network thinks are highly probable. And, we won't even search nodes for which the neural network thinks are completely garbage. So, this importance sampling trick, basically reduces the breadth of the tree from 361 to, you know, three or four, because the neural network is very confident that like, you know, only these three or four moves make any sense at all. Mhm. So, in this step, we're going to walk down the tree, and we select A, and then we traverse to that child. S gets updated to, S child, of, s, a. And then we repeat, until we run into a state, S, which, where we've ran out of tree, right? Like it's, it's, it has no children, we haven't expanded it yet. So, this brings us to step two, which is, expansion. So, expansion is, once we've ran out of tree, we've hit a leaf node, what do we do? We want to add this leaf, we want to add its children to the tree. So, we're going to update, our, tree, by adding children. Now, we don't necessarily have to, like, instantiate all the data structures for all 361 children because that would be very expensive. In fact, what you typically do is you, just instantiate a single parent, S, and we, we just store, we evaluate, with the neural network, the policy vector, at this leaf node. Now, because we have the policy vector, S, we can query this dynamically whenever we need, and so we only need to store the, the parent's state, S, and we don't need to populate all the children. We just basically query the, the probabilities whenever we need to do step one, right? So, so we'll just store the probability vector, P of S, A, at S, and we don't actually instantiate children. Wait, so sorry, if you don't instantiate children, how does S get updated to S child of S, A? Right. S child of S, A can be decided, so you, you have a Go engine which implements the logic of the game, and that's fully deterministic. So you don't need to save the state in the, in memory. You can just compute it dynamically when you, when you traverse down. Got it. Now, in practice, you might actually want to save some of these just so that you don't have to re-evaluate the Go rules, which can be somewhat expensive in Python. But conceptually, you don't need to save S in the, in memory. So, we run the neural net, on S leaf. We get, P, of S leaf. We also get, the value prediction, V of S leaf, which is, you know, this, am I going to win or lose. And, this, is step three, which is evaluation. Now, in the original AlphaGo paper, they had, they did two things. They actually did this value network evaluation, but they also did a rollout, where they took a very cheap, fast, linear policy, like 1,000 parameters, and played the game with this linear policy all the way to the end, and then they saw whether they won or lost. And they averaged V and, you know, this rollout value together. In AlphaGo Zero, they completely discarded rollouts, and they only used V, because they found that V was already so strong and accurate that the rollout actually added noise rather than signal. And, from an implementation standpoint, rollouts are a huge pain because you have to play the game hundreds of times in a Go rule engine, which can be slow, whereas evaluating a neural net is very fast and can be batched on a GPU. So, for, you know, for vibe coding this, we completely discard rollouts, and we only use V. Mhm. Okay. So, then this brings us to step four, which is backup. So, once we have V of S leaf, we want to, essentially, update the Q values of all the parents that we took to get here. So you remember that when we traversed down the tree, we visited a number of parent nodes, right? So we have S root, and we went here, and we went here, and we ended up at S leaf, right? So, this represents a path of states and actions. So, we're going to have S0, S1, S2, and so forth, all the way to S leaf, and we took actions, A0, A1, A2, to get here. So, what we're going to do is for all S, A in the path, we are going to increment the visit count, N(S, A) plus equals one. And we are going to update the mean action value, Q(S, A). Now, we talked about before, like, Q represents the average. So, how do we update the average? Well, it's just you take the current sum of Vs, which is just Q(S, A) times the old visit count, plus our new V of S leaf, and then you divide the whole thing by the new visit count. Wait, what is, what is V of S leaf? So, S leaf is this node that we just added to the tree. And we just evaluated with the neural network. So, we got a win probability of like 0.7. Mhm. And so, we're going to basically back up this win probability, 0.7, to all the parents that we took to get here. Oh, okay, wait, wait, so if we back it up, why is it V of S leaf? Is it always, so do you evaluate V at every parent node as well? No, so the parent nodes have already been evaluated in previous simulations, right? In this simulation, we started from S root, we walked down the tree, we went to S leaf, we evaluated S leaf with the neural net, and now we're going to back up that value of S leaf to all the parents. Got it. So, the parent's Q value is basically, you can think of it like the average of all of the leaf evaluations of its descendants. Got it, yes, okay, that makes sense. So, descendants are already, yeah, that makes sense. 'Cause they were evaluated when they were leaves. Exactly, yes, yes. Yes, exactly. So this is why, you know, the parent's Q value is sort of this, like, Monte Carlo average of the descendant leaves. Mhm. Now, there's a small detail which is that Go is an alternating game, right? One player goes, then another player goes, and so we want to maximize the win probability for our turn, and we want to minimize the win probability for our opponent's turn. So, in practice, you actually have to put, like, signs on these Vs, right? So you might, like, say, if V represents, "am I going to win from this state," then on our opponent's turn, we want to choose actions that minimize that. Mhm. Or, conversely, we want to maximize the probability that our opponent is going to lose. Mhm. So, in practice, you just put minus signs on this V as you, as you walk up. Yeah. And the tree is highly asymmetric, right? Like it has some branches that have been explored very deeply because the neural network thinks they're highly probable and they have high value. And other branches, we haven't explored at all. So, now we want to decide what move to make at S root. So, how do we decide which move to make? Well, you can think about how we have this probability vector of how often we visited each of these children, right? So we have N(S, A) for all A. And we're going to, basically, select the action that maximizes N(S, A). The visit count? Yes. So, why the visit count and not Q? Well, because the visit count, under PUCT, is actually a better, more stable indicator of what the best move is. If we've visited a node many, many times, it means that even after exploring it, it still had a very high value. If we had a node which had a very high Q, but we only visited it once, it means that we might have just got lucky on that one evaluation and we didn't actually explore it deeply enough to verify that it's actually good. Got it. So, the visit count is a very robust indicator of what the best move is. So, we're going to choose the action that maximizes N(S, A). And we're going to record this probability distribution over visits, so we're going to call this, like, Pi(A|S), which is just like N(S, A) normalized so that they all sum to one. And we're going to save this Pi and S in memory, because we're going to use this to train the next version of the neural network. Okay. And this is the core loop of AlphaGo: you play the game using this tree search, you record the visit counts, and then you train the neural network to predict the visit counts. Wait, what do you mean? You train the neural network to predict the visit counts? I thought you train it to predict the expert moves, or in tabula rasa, you train it to predict...? Right. Great question. So, in tabula rasa, you don't have expert moves. So, what is the policy target, right? Well, you can think about tree search as basically a policy improvement operator, right? We start with a neural net which has some policy, P(S, A). It's okay, but it's not great. We run tree search, which is very expensive, and we get a much better policy, Pi(A|S), which is based on, you know, looking ahead into the future. Mhm. So, we want to train the next version of the neural network to predict Pi(A|S) without doing the tree search. Oh, okay, wait. So you train the next neural network to directly predict what the previous neural network resolved with tree search? Exactly. And so, the next neural network's raw policy, P, will be as strong as the previous neural network's search policy, Pi. Mhm. And then you do tree search on top of that, and then it gets even stronger? Exactly, yes, yes. Yes, exactly. This is the policy iteration loop. That's beautiful. It's extremely beautiful. It's like you have a bootstrapping process where you start with a weak policy, you use search to make it stronger, you train a neural net to predict that stronger policy, and then you repeat. Mhm. And then it just keeps getting stronger? Yes, exactly. So, this is the policy iteration loop. And we're going to write down, you know, what the loss function is to train this network. So, we have two targets. We have S, and we have our target value, Z, which is whether we won or lost the game eventually, and we also have our target policy, Pi, which is this visit distribution. So, our loss function is we want to minimize, over our neural network parameters, theta, the squared error of the value prediction, V(S) minus Z. So, Z is like 1 or 0, depending on whether we won or lost. And then we also want to minimize the cross entropy between our policy prediction, P(S) and our target policy, Pi. So, this is sum over A, of Pi(A) times log P(A|S). This is a very standard multi-task loss function. We have a regression loss, and we have a classification loss. And we just, you know, do backpropagation to update theta. And that's literally the whole training loop. And so, how do you collect the data to train this? Well, you just have the neural network play against itself, right? So, you initialize your neural net, you have it play, you know, 10,000 games against itself, you record S, Z, and Pi for every move in every game, and then you train on that dataset. Mhm. So, this is the AlphaGo Zero loop. And this is what KataGo and, you know, other Go bots use. Now, in practice, KataGo added a number of really smart enhancements to this, which is why they were able to get a 40x reduction in compute. I'm not going to talk about all of them because, you know, there's quite a lot, but one of the main ones is that they found that you don't actually need to play the game all the way to the end to get Z. Mhm. Instead, you can use the value function itself to estimate, you know, who won or lost, and then you can kind of truncate the game early, and that saves you, like, half of the game playouts, which is a huge saving in compute. Another thing they did is they found that you don't actually need to do 2048 simulations on every move during self-play. In fact, on some moves, you can do very few simulations, like 100, and on other moves, you can do, like, many more, and that also saves you a lot of compute. So, there's a lot of these kind of, like, system-level optimizations that they did, which made it much more efficient. Mhm. Okay. So, this is the basic AlphaGo Zero algorithm. And I think this is a great place to stop and, you know, see if you have any questions, or if we should move on to, like, how you might actually implement this in code. Awesome. Okay. One question. What is—we had P_a as the probability of taking this action from the parent. What is the difference between P and Pi? Yes. Great question. So, P(S, A) is the raw neural network's prediction of what are good moves. This is, you know, before we do any search. Pi(A|S) is the visit distribution after we do search. So, Pi is basically a much better policy because it has looked ahead into the future. And we want to train the neural network—so we want to train P to predict Pi. Mhm. Got it. So, you can think about search as a sort of policy improvement operator, and then training as a sort of policy distillation step, where we distill the search policy back into the raw network. Got it. Yeah, that's beautiful. Yeah. Okay. So, let's talk about how you might actually write this in code. And I think, for your audience, they might be wondering, like, "Okay, well, is this something that I can actually do on my laptop, or do I need, like, a big GPU cluster?" And, as we mentioned before, thanks to, like, KataGo's optimizations, you can actually train a very strong Go bot on a single GPU in, you know, a few days. So, I'll write down what the main files are, and then how they interact. So, we have a file called Go Engine, which implements the rules of the game. So, we have S, we have A, we have checking if a move is valid, we have making a move, we have finding all captured stones, and so forth. And this, as we mentioned, is fully deterministic and can be implemented in Python. Now, Python is slow, so what most Go bots do is they write this in C++ or Rust and then wrap it in Python, and that makes it much faster. But, for small board sizes like 9 by 9, you can actually write this in pure Python and it's still, you know, fast enough. Mhm. Then, we have MCTS.py, which implements this four-step iterative process. So, we have selection, expansion, evaluation, backup. And this is a very standard recursive algorithm, so you can write this in a few hundred lines of Python. And, as we mentioned, Claude or ChatGPT can write this for you, and it's usually pretty, pretty accurate. Then, we have Net.py, which implements the neural network architecture. So, we have this ResNet, we have the two heads, and we use PyTorch to do this. PyTorch is great because it has, you know, very easy-to-use layers, and we can do training on a GPU very easily. And then finally, we have Train.py, which implements this policy iteration loop. So, we have self-play, we have recording the data, we have training the network, and then we repeat. Mhm. So, these are the four main files, and they interact in a very simple way. So, during self-play, Train.py is going to query MCTS.py to make moves. MCTS.py is going to query Go Engine to check if moves are valid, and it's also going to query Net.py to evaluate leaf nodes. And then, once the game ends, Train.py is going to save the data, and then it's going to update Net.py using this loss function. And then, we repeat. Mhm. And how long does this take to train? So, on a 9 by 9 board, which is much smaller than 19 by 19, you can train a superhuman 9 by 9 Go bot on a single GPU in about 24 hours. Because we know the policy network evaluated on the root node gives us, on a 3x3 board with one existing stone placed, eight possible children that this AI could take. So, with each of the children, the policy network also gives us the probability of selecting that child. So, the first step is to do the selection of the tree. And again, this is a very shallow tree. All we have so far is a tree of depth one, essentially. So, our first move is to select by maximizing or arg maxing the PUCT criteria, which is basically, you know, Q(s, a) plus, you know, c_puct times P divided by N over 1 plus N_a. So, for each of these, N_a is zero for all the actions initially, and N is zero. And so we're going to basically just, you know, pick according to this. Initially, what is going to be the chosen action here is most likely going to be biased towards the highest likelihood action here, right, because these are sort of uniform for everyone. So, let's suppose P1 was the highest probability node, so you selected this one here. Now you got to this node, and you realized that it's not a leaf node, right? There are more—it's not a terminal game, so you cannot resolve the final resolution. So the next step that you do is expansion. So, you will then run this node, this board state, through the policy network. Note that this is the AI's move, right? Like, the AI is making this move. And so when we expand this tree, we're now thinking about what the human might do, or any opponent might do, right? So, this is like your opponent. The tree expansion process actually is completely—so, when we evaluate the node here, we're going to now evaluate the node from the perspective of this player. Yep. So then this one has possible actions that we could take, and we expand basically the leaf nodes here. So, for each of these nodes that we could arrive at, we're going to now check how good those nodes are, right? So they'd be, from here like the human could play here, the human could play here, or the human could play here. And we're going to store essentially the v_theta for each of these things, so v_theta of node one, or node one prime, v_theta node one prime prime, v_theta node one prime prime prime. And so we're basically using our neural network to make an intuitive guess of how good is this board from the perspective of this player. Yeah. And, unfortunately, because it's a zero-sum game, it's easy to deduce that the value for this player at this step is just 1 minus the value from this perspective. So it's easy to flip the search process depending on which player you're at. And so, this is the expansion step. You've taken a non-leaf node and expanded it and evaluated the value, and this is essentially a quick guess as to like, if I were to play to the end, am I going to win or not, right? So you can almost think about v_theta as a shortcut for searching to the end of the tree for any given simulation. And then this is essentially the evaluation step, where we're evaluating the quality of each of these boards. In the original AlphaGo Lee, they actually did something kind of interesting, which is that they took this value and they averaged it with the value of a real playout. So they actually played a real game from here all the way to the end. I'm just going to draw this squiggly line to indicate some path, and they kind of play this all the way to Tromp-Taylor resolution of the full board. And so this is like a zero or one, right? And so they took this value and they just averaged it with this one here. So the formula they did was like alpha times v_theta of some node, plus sort of 1 minus alpha of a true randomly sampled playout. And you might be wondering, like, "Okay, well, how do they play this out, right?" Like it would be very, very costly to do another search on this playout, like almost like a tree within a tree. So they don't do this; instead, they just take the policy network and play it against itself. So they just take this as both players, and they just play it all the way to the end. And this is something that helps ground the estimates here in reality, because you can get a single-sample estimate of whether you win or not. You can think about in the endgame where the board is almost resolved that this one actually becomes quite useful, because the play according to the policy will most likely decide a pretty reasonable guess of the game. And so you're not, you know, facing a problem where this one kind of becomes untethered from reality. It turns out this is totally unnecessary. So, in all subsequent papers after AlphaGo Lee, they just got rid of this. Yeah, yeah. And so in my implementation I also did the same, and it speeds things up a lot because you don't have to roll these games out on every single simulation. Yeah, yeah. Okay, so, again, just to reinforce my own understanding and just to re-explain it—for the audience, by the way, in case it's not obvious, the P there in the 'select' is the probability coming from the network in this case? Correct, the policy network here. Yeah. Okay, so, fundamentally, a simulation—just think of it as like rolling out one more node in this search process, almost? So, a simulation is easy to think about when the whole tree already exists, right? You just walk down the tree using the PUCT selection criteria, and you keep going. Yeah. Now, in AlphaGo, the data structure is such that we begin with a tree that has basically only depth one, which is its only children, and you want to iteratively build out the tree as you're also selecting actions down the tree. Yep. So that's the kind of core thing here: because Go is such a combinatorially complex game, you cannot afford to build the tree in advance and then search it. You must search while building the tree. Right, okay. So, let me just finish up with actually the last step, which is the backup, right? So once you've scored these things, you basically take the Q-value assigned to the node here for taking this action, which is now just the average across your evaluated values here. You take a running mean over all of the simulations that you've taken, and they average the values of the children nodes. Yep. So, that's what is known as the backup step. And once you evaluate this, you can actually kind of recursively go back. So if you know the action value of this node, you can then take the average on its parent, and so forth. So, you have this kind of four-step process where you are choosing the best action that you know of so far, then you may run into a node where you haven't been to before, so you need to grow the tree a bit, and then you run it through the network to guess whether you're going to win or not, and then you walk all the way back up to the root node to update your values on what the best moves are. So as you do this iteratively, this selection criteria will cause you to visit the—because you're always selecting according to this criteria, you're always going to be selecting the best action you think at any given branch, right? So, the final visit counts of how often you chose these things will reflect your correct policy distribution as induced through this search process. And so, the visit count that we store in the node earlier actually becomes the sort of vote for which way we should finally select an action here. Yeah, yeah. So, you know, as a sort of test of understanding, it's worth thinking a little bit about whether we could make this even simpler, right? Like, could we actually maybe even get rid of this one and still make the thing work? So, recall that when you do an expansion and then an evaluation at, let's say, this node, you are checking the sort of win probability at each of the child nodes, right? And so if this one is, you know, like one, and these are zero, you do kind of know something about which action might be better to take. And so why would you still need this, right? Like, why not just normalize this one into some distribution and call that your policy distribution? This is fine, you can do this, and this probably does work. But in practice, having a single forward pass that gives you a pretty good guess is how the breadth is pruned out. There is a sort of duality here. Like, it would be weird if, let's say, the policy recommended an action that disagreed with the value, right? If, let's say, the policy said this was a very high probability, but this one said it was a low value, then there's actually something kind of fundamentally wrong between your policy head and your value head. So they are linked, and you probably could get rid of this if you came up with a different way to recover this from just the value evaluations. Right. But just to make sure I understand, the reason you don't do that is so that you don't have to do 360 independent forward passes to say, 'Hey, here's the value of everything, let's arg max over it,' right? Instead, you can just do one forward pass and get the probabilities of all of them. You can usually batch these somewhat efficiently, so it probably is not a huge computational burden in practice. But yes, you would have to pass up to 361 boards into a single mini-batch update to evaluate all the values here, and then normalize them. Now, there's actually a more important reason why we still do this, which is how Monte Carlo tree search is used to feed back on itself, and sort of recursively improve its own predictions and search capabilities. And that's where this—having this as an explicit entity you're modeling, rather than an implicit normalization over your value—is a good idea. Makes sense. Okay. Okay, so, we talked about the simulations and basically, you know, what you end up with as you roll out the number of simulations is a tree that kind of looks like... I'm drawing a very low-dimensional version of this. Of course, in the real game it's much more high-dimensional, but you'll end up with basically a tree structure that has a lot of leaves that kind of terminate and are not visited again because their value is deemed to be too low. But then, you know, along one path, there will be a set of actions with very, very high visit counts that kind of gravitate towards that one set of decisions as you increase N. Yeah. So, this is kind of like the mental picture of what the tree in Monte Carlo tree search looks like. And you should contrast this with an exhaustive tree, like in tic-tac-toe where you could say like, you know, there's nine actions, and then eight, and then seven, and then six, and so it's a sort of like nine-factorial-sized tree. The Monte Carlo tree search in Go is very, very sparse, right? It only considers the paths that you've expanded child nodes on. Okay, so now that we have the search algorithm that applies the value function as well as the policy function, we can now talk about how the Monte Carlo tree search algorithm can actually act as an improvement operator on top of these guys here. So, the beauty of how AlphaGo trains itself is that it actually can take this final search process, the outcome of the search process, and tell the policy network, "Hey, instead of having MCTS do all this legwork to arrive here, why don't you just predict that from the get-go?" right? Like, "Why don't you not use this guess and just predict this to begin with?" And if you have this guess to begin with in your policy network, then MCTS has to do a lot less work to get things to work. So, the idea of MCTS is very brilliant, which is like: we got something better by applying search, and we're going to now on our next iteration of updating this network, just train this to approximate the outcome of 1,000 steps of search. Yep. And so, instead of starting here, we get to now have a neural network start here, and then, you know, the play gets stronger once we then apply another 1,000 steps on top of it. And you can keep going, right? So, the training algorithm for AlphaGo is to basically take the games where you've applied this search on every move that the policy encountered, whether you won or lost—and that's quite important—and you're just going to train the model to imitate the search process. So, there's an analogy to robotics, actually, which is the DAgger algorithm. First, I'm going to draw a schematic of, let's say, the states, right? So S0, S1, S2, S3. So, let's say we took a series of actions in an MDP to get a trajectory, and these actions may be suboptimal, right? Maybe we lost at the end of this game. So, there is a family of algorithms that basically take trajectories and relabel the actions to better trajectories. So, maybe a better action here would have been to take, you know, A0 prime, a better action here would have been to take A1 prime, and then yet another one like A2 prime, A3 prime. So, what MCTS is doing is basically saying: you played this game where you eventually lost, but on every single action, I'm going to give you a strictly better action that you should take instead. It does not guarantee that you are going to win, but it does guarantee that, you know, if you take these tuples as training data—so that you retrain your policy network to predict these ones instead of those ones—you're going to do better. And this is very related to DAgger in robotics and imitation learning, where you want to collect an intervention here, and even if you're in a not-great state—for example, like a self-driving car that, you know, veers off the side of the road—there is still a valid action that kind of corrects you and brings you back. Right. Okay, so, pedantic question. But is there a guarantee that MCTS must be better than the policy? For example, you could imagine early on in training, because MCTS is informed by the value network, early on in training when the value network hasn't been well trained on finished games, that MCTS is worse than a sort of randomized policy. So, is it just like a heuristic that MCTS is better than the policy? Or is there some guarantee? Right. In practice, it is a heuristic, and it does work in practice. But let me illustrate an example where MCTS can give you a worse distribution than your policy network. This can often happen if your self-play algorithm has trained to a good point, but then somehow it collapses because it's not trained on diverse data or something. So, let's say we have a board state where the policy recommendations here are very good, so like Pi(A|S) is great. But somehow—because maybe we're playing on a lot of games where the bots just resign instead of playing all the way to the Tromp-Taylor resolution—they kind of forget how to evaluate those kind of late-stage playouts, right? Like in the case that we showed with the corner play, maybe like 100% of our training data in our replay buffer has lost examples of how to evaluate the value function at those states. So, you might end up in a scenario where your terminal value is like very bad. And if the terminal values of the leaves are not good, then this will actually probably propagate all the way up and cause your PUCT selection criteria and your backups to be off, and then you'll end up visiting a very, very different distribution than what your policy initially recommended. Also, if your number of sims is low, then you might also have a variance issue where you just don't explore enough, right? Like, it's only guaranteed to converge when you kind of take N to infinity. So, variance in, you know, your search process as well as inaccuracies in your evaluation can definitely screw with the quality of your policy network recommendation. And so that's why it's not a guarantee to improve. And that is why I suspect AlphaGo Lee had the playouts to the end in their training algorithm, so that they could ground this thing in real playouts. In practice, what you could also do is just, like, for 10% of the games, you prevent the bots from resigning and you just say, 'Resolve it to the end.' So you get some training data in your replay buffer to really resolve those kind of late-stage playouts that normal human players would kind of not play to. Yeah, yeah. And when you train multiple best response policies, you can basically then distill the RL algorithms into the labels for a given opponent. So you might have, let's say, a best response policy against $\pi_B$, and then maybe you have a collect- a league of, you know, of opponents, like $\pi_B, \pi_C, \pi_D$. Wait, this is a sort of tangential question, but why is it okay to have a buffer in AlphaGo? Because every time I talk to any researcher here, they're telling me about how bad it is to be off-policy. So why is that okay? Great question, yeah. This gets into the sort of fundamental off-policy versus on-policy reinforcement learning questions. As you recall, in MCTS, you take actions that you took, and you relabel them to different actions on the same states, right? So, the off-policy part here comes where, what if you're relabeling states that your new policy would never visit? What's the point? You're kind of wasting capacity, and in the extreme limit, imagine your distribution of states in your training buffer are all states that you would never visit. Then you're basically supervising them to take good actions on states you would never achieve, and therefore, your policy can get really bad. So this is where off-policy can really hurt AlphaGo. However, if you interpret this from the DAgger perspective—which is basically a way to correct yourself back to the optimal trajectory, given some data—what you want in an algorithm like this is to have mostly states that you would visit. But then you have a small percentage, or maybe a reasonable percentage, of states in this kind of high-dimensional tube around your optimal trajectories, and any of those states are given a supervision target to kind of funnel you back into your optimal trajectory. So maybe I can just draw it real quickly here. Great. So in a DAgger-style setup, what your optimal training data distribution is, is that here are your optimal states and actions. This is like, you know, you want to be in this state, you want to be in this state, you want to be in this state, and then you win here. And then these are your optimal policy actions. These are the things that you definitely want to train on, but to make it robust to disturbances, you want to make sure that if you happen to drift off into some other states, you can kind of funnel yourself back into... But why isn't this a fully general argument for off-policy training? This is actually why you want to do off-policy training sometimes. You don't want to have a compounding error where if you make a mistake, you don't have the data of how to return back to your optimal distribution. And so, optimal control does not really say too much about how to not accidentally get here, because it's making the assumption that once you learn the policy you'll never get here. But in applications like robotics, a gust of wind blows you slightly off, and then now you need to correct. Or the friction on one of your tires is lower than the other wheel, and now your car is drifting and you've got to correct it. These kind of things in more real environments often happen. Actually, there's a funny quote about chess and also Go: "the problem with Go and chess is that the other player is always trying to do some shit." So, things can kind of drift off, and you always want to be able to correct back to your winning condition. So, your replay buffer really should have the states that your policy would visit plus some distribution of states that you might drift to, and then how to return back to your optimal states. Now, if you take this to the extreme, and you say, "well, we don't have any of this data, and we're going to just be labeling with MCTS states that are so far away from our optimal behavior, like this bag of states over here," well, yeah, each of them gets an MCTS label, and your policy learns how to take the best possible action here, but you never get here. You're training your model on states you would never reach. This is a problem, and this is where off-policy can really hurt. Mhm. So actually, as part of this project, I did try an experiment where I took a bunch of trajectories. To try to saturate the GPU as much as possible, what I did was I took random states from the dataset and reran MCTS on just those states. So, instead of playing a whole game where I'm doing MCTS on every move, I just ignore the causality of moves, pick random board states, and label those with my current network. I might revisit old states that I've labeled before and relabel them again with my current network. In practice, this actually does work. You can take some states that are reasonable and constantly be relabeling them while we're training. This actually starts to converge on a very robotics-like setup, which is very common, where you have your dataset of trajectories, and then you have something like a replay buffer pusher. These are off-policy, offline trajectories. So, your replay buffer pusher pushes transition tuples to the replay buffer, and then you have some job that's continuously replanning what the best action you should have taken instead of this action is. In robotics, it's actually very common to minimize TD error. Your Bellman updater constantly pulls things from here and tries to satisfy the Q(s, a) backup as r + gamma * max_a Q(s', a). From here, you have your trainer, which is trying to fit the state to action, or fit the Q to the Q target. So here, you can think about this as a sort of planner. You visit old states that you've been to, take your current model, and rethink what you could have done better if you visited them. This is actually how off-policy robotics learning systems are usually trained. These things are just a simpler recipe, but in the Google QT-Opt days we did things like this. Where is the trainer? Oh yeah, the trainer is you trying to minimize Q(s, a) and the Q target. Wait, so can you explain the whole setup again, like at the high level? Yep. So, you have your off-policy data that came from various policies, you're constantly pushing transitions that you saw before to a replay buffer, and then you've got this thing called a Bellman updater, which basically replans what action you should have taken at state s to have a better value, instead of the action you took. And the way you enforce that is you try to minimize the TD error. Actually, given this, you have s'. You compute Q of s', and you find the action that should go with s' that makes this Q value as high as possible. And then you add that to the reward here, and that gives you your actual target. So, for this current s and a, your Q target is this. Now you send back the Q target to this transition, so with this tuple, you pair a Q target. And then here on the trainer, you simply use supervised learning and you minimize your current network's Q(s, a) with its target. God, okay. So, in the background you're just like, "hey, let me basically think through how valuable were all these actions actually." Yeah, in a more optimal policy where you're trying to maximize this, what is the Q target of this transition? It's sort of like basically daydreaming. Exactly, yeah. You can think about it like going back in hindsight and being like, "hmm, given what I've seen in the historical buffer, was there a better action I could have taken?" Now, the connection to Go here that I tried—and it was moderately successful but too complex to open-source—was that you replace this with an MCTS relabeler where, instead of doing this kind of target network computation, you run MCTS on your transition. So in this case, you have your state, your action, and then whether you won or not at the end. Actually, you can just toss those last two; you don't care about them. You just take your state, and you plan MCTS to get your best policy, pi, on your current network—not the network that took this action, but your current best policy network. You just rerun your search offline on these transitions. And, if these are transitions that your policy can get to, then this actually acts as a very nice stabilizing effect. Also, the one other benefit is that you can fully saturate your GPU better because you're not blocking on the Go game to give you board states; you just simply search across all board states at any depth in parallel. So then here, the trainer would just predict the MCTS label as much as possible. Again, this kind of works, and this is quite relevant in robotics where you really just will have a lot of offline data and you can't simulate things like MCTS. But in practice, it does run into the problem where if the current model is looking at states that it would never reach, then it's kind of wasting capacity. So, you have to be a little bit careful here. So the on-policy thing, and also much of RL, has kind of converged to a much more on-policy setup, where they don't really try to directly train on off-policy data. At best they use off-policy data as a way to reduce variance, but not directly influence the objective. And why have they converged to that? It's just more stable. Yeah, so you might use the off-policy Q as a way to do advantage computation, like Q minus the sum of Q over N actions. That's kind of like your average value, and this is your current Q value, so your advantage for that action is like the average value minus your current one. So, people can try to estimate Q in an off-policy way and then just use advantage here, and then if there's a problem in these dynamics, it doesn't blow up your loss as much. And so in robotics, there's a kind of convergence towards using off-policy data to just shape your rewards but not actually be directly your objective. I'm reminded now of our earlier conversation of why MCTS is so favorable as compared to the kind of REINFORCE or policy gradient kind of things that LMs do. This might be totally wrong, but I wrote a blog post a few months ago about how RL, at least policy gradient RL, is even more inefficient than you might think. The inefficiency one thinks about naively is the fact that you have to roll out a whole trajectory in order to get any learning signal at all. As these trajectories become longer and longer, instead of just previously completing the next word in the sentence, an agent has to spend two days' worth of work to figure out if it did this project correctly. The amount of information per FLOP has been decreasing, as you have to roll out two days' worth of thinking in order to see if you even did something correctly to implement this feature. The amount of samples per FLOP has been decreasing. But you can think of it as trying to maximize your learning bits per FLOP, right? And you can think of bits per FLOP as samples per FLOP times bits per sample. What I just mentioned a second ago is that the samples per FLOP go down as RL becomes more and more long-horizon. But at least this kind of naive RL is also terrible from a bits per sample perspective, at least compared to supervised learning. So early on in training, let's say you have a vocabulary size for an LM that is 100k long, so there's 100k possible tokens that one can answer. And you have a totally untrained model, and you have a prompt like "the sky is...". With supervised learning, what would happen is that the model would have some probability distribution over all the things it could say, there's a label that says actually the term here is "blue," and it would learn basically through cross-entropy loss exactly how far its distribution is from correctly saying "blue." Now, if you were doing this through RL, the model would try, "the sky is halcyon," nope, that's wrong; "the sky is tall," nope, that's wrong. It's a totally untrained model, right? And so it would have to do this on the order of 100,000 times in order to just stumble on "blue," and then get some learning signal off of that. If you're in the supervised learning regime, you have your distribution of probabilities, you get told that it's "blue" and you figure out how far off you are. The amount you learn is a function of your pass rate, so the further away you are from "blue," the more you've learned to go towards "blue" using cross-entropy loss. You can think of it as your pass rate, your prior probability of having said "blue," and as a function of that, in supervised learning through cross-entropy loss, you're learning... You want to learn negative log p—p being the pass rate—bits once you get this label. Whereas in RL, if you're just randomly guessing and seeing if it works or not, that's just basically going to be the entropy of a binary random variable, which is... And what's also tough here is that actually the distribution that you're sampling under is your policy's distribution. Right. So it's like if your policy has no chance of sampling "blue," then you will never get a signal. Exactly. Right. So that's being modeled by the fact that your probability of sampling "blue" is extremely low. If you do sample it, you do learn as much as you would have learned in supervised learning. In all other cases—like 99.999% of the time in an untrained model—you're just learning incredibly little from seeing that "halcyon" is not the correct word or "tall" is not the correct word. And that's what happens most of the time. So, if you try to graph this, putting your pass rate on the x-axis, and here you put the bits you're learning from a sample. If you have 0% here, 50% here, and 100% here, at the end of training you're here. If you have supervised learning, the negative log of the pass rate would look something like this. And then, the entropy of a binary random variable would look like this, depending on whether you're doing nats or bits. Yeah, if you do bits, it's like 1 right here at the peak. This is like a coin flip; you learn the most from a coin flip. This is supervised learning, this is RL. However, the problem is you spend most of training in this regime, right, in the low pass rate regime. And, in fact, if how fast you're learning is a function of how many bits per sample you're getting, and you're getting very little signal here, let's chart the pass rate on a log scale. If you put the x-axis on a log scale where at the beginning of training with a vocab size of 100k, the pass rate is 1/100,000, then 1/10,000, 1/1,000, 1/100—then, okay, what this graph looks like is where supervised learning would look like this, and RL would look like that. Yeah, and arguably, you spend all your time here. Yeah, exactly. Potentially never even getting a single success, right? Why is AlphaGo an elegant RL algorithm? The major reason is that you never have to initialize at a 0% success rate and solve the exploration problem of how to get a non-zero success rate. And this is what allows you to hill climb this beautiful supervised learning signal. If you look at the actual implementation of AlphaGo, every step of the way, there's actually no TD error learning or dynamic programming, at least explicitly. It's just supervised learning on a value classification as well as a policy KL minimization. So, it's just a supervised learning problem on improved labels, and so the training is very stable, right? You can train as big of a network as you want, you can retrain this on the dataset, and everything will just go stably. The infrastructure is very simple to implement as well; you don't need a complex distributed system to keep everything on-policy. At the end of the day, you're just saying, "I have some improved labels, let's retrain my supervised model on these targets." Yeah, yeah. And so you're always in this beautiful regime where you're just trying to improve the policy, rather than trying to escape a local minimum where every signal is flat all around you. One thing I really wanted to talk to you about is that you did a bunch of the research for this project through this kind of automated, LLM coding assistant loop. And there's an idea that if you fully automated AI research, you could have some sort of singularity. Obviously we're not there yet, but to the extent that we have early indications of what this process might look like, I am curious what your observations are about what the AI is good at, what it's not good at, what you think about this scenario's likelihood eventually, and what thoughts you have about this in general. For sure, yeah. I think automated scientific research is one of the most exciting skills that the frontier labs are developing right now. And I think it's important for everyone who's doing any kind of research to get a good intuition of what it can do now and what it can't, and how the science process might work in the future once we have AIs automating a lot of this investigation. So, in brief, I mostly used Opus 4.6 and 4.7 throughout working on this, and what works is that the models can do a very good job of doing hyperparameter optimization. In the past, people would come up with a search space of hyperparameters, like learning rate, weight decay, and maybe how many layers are in your network, and they would just do a grid search or a sort of Bayesian hyperparameter optimization approach, and then it would find some tuned parameters. The really cool thing that automated coding can do now is that it can search a much more open-ended set of problems, right? It can say, "Well, I've identified that the gradients are kind of small on this layer, so let me change it up here. Let me rewrite the code so the data loader has a new augmentation I came up with. Let's try to find the best way to fit the constraints of the optimization problem." And you end up with this much more flexible and high-level, almost grad-student-like ability to just grind a performance metric. And so this can squeeze out quite a lot of performance. On a fixed dataset with a fixed time budget, you can improve perplexity by quite a lot on a classification problem like LLMs or Go. And it is also fantastic now at basically executing any experiment, right? So I have a Claude skill that I wrote called "experiment," where I give it a description of what I wanted to plot. I just describe, "Here's the x-axis I want, here's the y-axis, answer this question for me," and it'll run off and do all the experiments, compile the plot, make a report, and suggest what might have caused it or so forth. So, that's what works quite well today, and I think we can expect that these abilities get better in the future. But it's also useful to know what it was not doing so well today. On my blog version of this tutorial, I have a plot of basically all the experiments I did, grouped in a sort of tree, where every node represents a failed, successful, or mixed experimental result, and from there it branches off into a child representing the follow-on experiment. Occasionally, I will rabbit hole down a track like this off-policy MCTS relabeling, do a few experiments, and then realize it's probably not worth it, so then I'll jump to a completely different track. And I call these things rows. So, what I find is that current closed models that the public can access today don't seem to be that great at selecting what the next experiment should be in a given track, and they don't seem to be able to step back and do the lateral thinking of, "Wait a minute, this track doesn't really make sense. Let's go back to first principles and think about what the bottleneck might be or what we are trying to achieve, right?" And so, often, I had to catch infra bugs myself by prompting the right question to Claude, like, "Investigate what is causing this discrepancy," and then it'll answer the question. Having worked at different AI labs and played around with parallel agents trying different ideas, what is your sense of how parallelizable AI innovation is? Yeah, great question. I think the research taste for executing well on the bitter lesson is knowing how much the bitter lesson can buy you, and how much is too much to ask for at any given moment. In the fullness of time, compute is the single most important determinant of how things work. It's almost inevitable that as you scale up energy, compute, and parameters, intelligence will just fall out of that. That's super beautiful and profound; no algorithmic detail really matters beyond that. But in the present day, we don't have infinite compute, infinite parameters, or an arbitrarily good initialization. We have to come up with heuristics that give us that, but these heuristics are probably somewhat redundant. That is probably why you see this effect where a lot of these compute multipliers don't necessarily stack—they might have some correlated benefit. Three years down the line, when NVIDIA GPUs have gotten even stronger, maybe they will stack even less well. At any given point in time, the benefit of a given compute multiplier is transitory, which is what I suspected with the KataGo paper. There were many algorithmic ideas applied, but with modern Blackwell and Ada-class GPUs—which are much better than the V100 GPUs used in that paper—you can see that some of these algorithmic tricks to speed up convergence just don't matter as much compared to other things. I think that's a matter of taste in the present time. Yeah, interesting. How about the outer loop? How verifiable is it for making AI smarter? So, how good is the outer verification loop for AI self-improvement, and does that matter? Yeah, I'm going to give a non-rigorous argument, but one that I intuitively believe. DeepMind, the AI research lab, started with a focus on games. They used games as their outer loop, and their researchers learned from the experience of solving games. Now they're working on LLMs, and presumably, there was some positive transfer from their time working on games like Atari, Go, and StarCraft that helps them make good LLMs today. I assume there is positive transfer in some regard, whether it's coding, general research ability, or project management. All these things probably help them do well. If that's the case, why wouldn't it also be true for automated AI researchers? They should be able to positively transfer experience from tackling quick-to-verify, quick-to-iterate-on environments to something more ambitious and economically useful, like automating drug discovery. I mean, I don't know. Hasn't the issue historically, at least until Gemini 1.5, been that a couple of years ago people were saying, "Look, Google isn't catching up in LLMs because they're too tied to the old approach"? There are games, but there are also ways in which that actively hinders you, so it's actually not obvious to me that there is positive transfer. The jury is still out, right? Who knows if—let's say Google is currently doing quite well—who knows if the initialization on training on games is ultimately going to hobble their ability to be the winner in the long term? It's hard to say for sure. Likewise, who knows if the seeming late start was really just them pre-training for longer on how to scale up TPUs? They invested all their tech tree in getting TPUs to be good, which seemed not that useful in the short term, but in the long term, it becomes a major advantage. It's even hard for humans to reason about what the optimal research strategy should be, even with the data we have today. Yeah, yeah, yeah. Cool. Okay, we should let people know how they can find out more about this project—whether to fork it themselves, or to check out your blog, where you've done an excellent job explaining many of these ideas. Where do people go next? Right. My website is evjang.com. There is a blog post there that links to an interactive version of this tutorial. On my GitHub, where the username is just ericjang, there is the AutoGo repo that people can fork and use to reproduce the training results. I also highly recommend people check out the blog post "As Rocks May Think." We touched on some of the ideas in this conversation, but it's this grander thesis of what happens when you have thinking as a primitive in... Computer science. Exactly. I highly recommend people check out that blog post as well. Yeah, and I encourage the audience to think about the relationship between thinking and Go via MCTS and search, and how it relates to LLMs. I think there's something quite profound there, and probably under-explored just because Go has been relatively under-explored compared to the boom in LLMs. It's not to say that I think we should have trees in our LLMs, but there is some very interesting duality between them. You can actually do a lot of research on Go, MCTS, and reasoning with very small budgets, so that's very exciting. Cool. Awesome, Eric. Thanks for doing this. It's an honor to be on the podcast.

Washington Today (5-15-26): Pres. Trump talks about Taiwan, Iran, trade on flight back from China

C-SPAN(1.9M)·57:55·5.0K views·2026-05-15
Donald TrumpPresident of the United States Xi JinpingPresident of China Bill CassidyU.S. Senator, Louisiana
TL;DR"Would the U.S.
President Trump returned from a Beijing summit without making explicit commitments regarding Taiwan’s defense or Iran, while the U.S. House passed a $157 billion military spending bill after soundly rejecting a Republican effort to defund NATO infrastructure. Taiwan...

TL;DR: President Trump returned from a Beijing summit without making explicit commitments regarding Taiwan’s defense or Iran, while the U.S. House passed a $157 billion military spending bill after soundly rejecting a Republican effort to defund NATO infrastructure.

Foreign Policy and the China Summit

  • Taiwan Ambiguity: President Trump told reporters aboard Air Force One that he made "no commitment either way" on supporting Taiwan’s independence or defending the island militarily.
      • Arms Sales: Trump is weighing a $14 billion weapons sale to Taiwan, noting he discussed the matter in "great detail" with President Xi Jinping but will make his own decision.
      • Strategic Deterrence: Trump declined to say if the U.S. would intervene in a conflict, stating, "There’s only one person that knows that... Me."
  • Iran and the Strait of Hormuz: The President claimed a U.S. blockade has "essentially wiped out" Iran’s armed forces.
      • China’s Role: Trump stated he did not ask President Xi for "favors" regarding Iran, arguing that since China receives 40% of its oil from the Strait and the U.S. receives none, it is in Xi’s own interest to pressure Iran to keep the waterway open.
  • Economic Agreements: Trump touted "billion-dollar days" in Beijing, including a deal for Boeing to sell China 200 planes (with a promise of up to 750) and hundreds of General Electric engines.
  • Human Rights: Trump reported that President Xi said releasing pro-democracy advocate Jimmy Lai would be "tough," though Xi is "strongly considering" the release of an underground church pastor.

Military Spending and NATO Infrastructure

  • FY2027 Appropriations: The House passed a $157 billion spending bill for military construction and Veterans Affairs in a 400-15 vote, marking the first appropriations bill of the cycle.
  • NATO Funding Dispute: An amendment by Rep. Greg Steube (R-FL) to shift $482 million from NATO infrastructure to domestic U.S. bases failed 80-333.
      • "America First" Argument: Steube argued that allies like France and Spain "subvert" U.S. efforts against Iran by denying airspace and should not receive American taxpayer subsidies.
      • Bipartisan Opposition: Rep. Debbie Wasserman Schultz (D-FL) argued that the cuts would force the U.S. to shoulder the full cost of bases that currently receive 85% of their funding from allies.
  • Army Budget Shortfall: Army Secretary Daniel Driscoll and Acting Chief of Staff Gen. Christopher Leneve denied reports of a $4–$6 billion shortfall requiring training cuts. They attributed budget pressures to the war in Iran and missions at the southern U.S. border but expressed optimism about being "backfilled" by the Department of Homeland Security.

Domestic Politics and Redistricting

  • Louisiana Senate Primary: Incumbent Senator Bill Cassidy (R-LA) faces a high-stakes primary against Trump-endorsed Rep. Julia Letlow and State Treasurer John Fleming.
      • Retribution Tour: Analysts suggest Cassidy is a target for his 2021 vote to convict Trump during the impeachment trial.
      • Logistical Chaos: The primary is being held on a Saturday under a new, "messy" closed system following ongoing legal battles over the state’s redistricting.
  • Tennessee Retirement: Rep. Steve Cohen (D-TN) announced he will not seek re-election after his majority-black district was redrawn by the GOP-controlled legislature. Cohen called the new map a "lion’s mouth," noting it was specifically designed to defeat him.

Public Safety and Health

  • Parental Prosecution in D.C.: U.S. Attorney Janine Pirro announced a new policy to aggressively prosecute parents of minors involved in "teen takeovers" and curfew violations in Washington, D.C.
      • Legal Penalties: Parents could face up to six months in prison for "contributing to the delinquency of a minor" if they fail to supervise their children.
  • Global Virus Outbreaks:
      • Ebola: A new outbreak in the Democratic Republic of Congo has resulted in 246 suspected cases and 65 deaths.
      • Hantavirus: The CDC is monitoring 41 people across 16 U.S. states following an outbreak linked to a South American cruise ship. CDC Director Dr. Jay Bhattacharya emphasized that there are currently no cases within the U.S. and the risk to the public remains "extremely low."
"Would the U.S. defend Taiwan if it came to it? I don't want to say. I’m not going to say that. There's only one person that knows that. You know who it is? Me." — President Donald Trump
"Alliances are not entitlement programs, nor are they blank checks. They are solemn commitments built on mutual defense, sacrifice, and responsibility." — Rep. Greg Steube (R-FL)
"Starting today, my office will aggressively prosecute parents under D.C.'s curfew law... if you drop your kid off and you fail to supervise them... you are going to face fines, court-ordered classes, and possible jail time." — U.S. Attorney Janine Pirro
"Do the right thing. Always." — Former UVA President Jim Ryan (at 2026 Commencement)

Eighty House Republicans voted on Friday, writes the Washington Examiner, for a measure to cut nearly $482 million from the military budget for the North Atlantic Treaty Organization and put the money into American military bases. The NATO Security Investment Program is a pool of money that all 32 countries in the alliance contribute to, funding digital and infrastructure projects. The U.S. and Germany are the biggest contributors to the program. That was from the Washington Examiner. This amendment was offered today by Congressman Greg Steube, Republican of Florida, to the fiscal year 2027 military construction and Veterans Affairs appropriations bill. It failed 80 to 333. Here's Congressman Steube offering the amendment. "I rise today to offer an amendment that puts the defense of our own troops, bases, and national security above all else. Not to subsidize the defenses of NATO countries that lecture us on peace, hesitate to stand with us, and in some cases actively undermine our efforts to confront the terrorist regime in Iran." "My amendment redirects more than 480 million in funding from the NATO Security Investment Program. Essentially, it moves nearly half a billion dollars used for foreign military base infrastructure and redirects it toward improving the infrastructure needs of our own bases, for our own service members, and for our own national security." "This is the definition of America First. The United States cannot continue carrying the defense burden for allied nations that fail to carry their own weight in the collective defense of each NATO member. But most importantly, we cannot continue to treat the generosity of the American taxpayer as automatic, while our allies treat their obligations as optional." "Operation Epic Fury has exposed why this amendment is necessary. When President Trump moved to confront the Iranian terror regime, some NATO countries chose to tarnish the meaning of alliance and tried to subvert our ability to target Iran." "Namely, it is France and Spain that continue to deny our military from using their airspace and many key defense facilities, forcing American forces to work around the very allied infrastructure that American taxpayers have helped sustain through NATO funding." "Many of these bases exist within the broader NATO architecture supported by programs like the NATO Security Investment Program, which this bill gives nearly half a billion dollars to. Yet when the United States called for support, our so-called allies chose silence over solidarity." "Alliances are not entitlement programs, nor are they blank checks. They are solemn commitments built on mutual defense, sacrifice, and responsibility. So if any foreign nation thinks they are deserving of the United States military and the backing of our taxpayers, best they not demand the comfort of our alliance and reject the courage it requires." Congressman Greg Steube, Republican of Florida, on the House floor. Congresswoman Debbie Wasserman Schultz, Democrat of Florida, spoke against the amendment. "This amendment would completely eliminate funding in the bill for the NATO Security Investment Program. The base bill already underfunds NATO infrastructure by $122 million less compared to the president's budget request." "And even President Trump recognized the value of this program and the return on investment that the NSIP program provides for the American taxpayer. The president's budget requests $604 million for the NATO Security Investment Program, which is the amount required to fully fund America's commitments towards NATO infrastructure investments." "The base bill provides only $482 million for the program, and this amendment would take that number all the way to zero. As the war in Ukraine rages on, this is not the time to abandon deterrence in Europe and undermine our alliances in the region." "Moreover, these investments are not only needed to meet NATO's requirements to deter aggression from our adversaries, but also to directly support U.S. troops stationed in Europe." "Around one-third of active NATO Security Investment Program projects are at U.S. theater posture locations, and an even larger number indirectly support U.S. troops stationed in or moving throughout the region. Yet the United States' share of funding for these projects is only 15 percent." "If the United States were to pay a share proportional to our gross national income, at the same rate as our allies, we would pay for around 47 percent of these projects. To cut funding for this program is not only not fiscally sound policy." "Without the NATO Security Investment Program, the operational requirements for these projects would not go away, and many of these projects would become a U.S. military construction burden." Congresswoman Debbie Wasserman Schultz, Democrat of Florida, on the House floor. This amendment failed by a vote of 80 "yes" and 333 "no." Here is Governor Spanberger with that swamp story. "Which reminds me of the time that I ended up in a swamp. Stick with me on this one. That wasn't a political reference, I mean a literal swamp. Early in my career at CIA, I went on one of my many training exercises, and the premise was very simple." "My colleagues and I needed to get from Point A to Point B. Our training facilitators gave us a compass, lined us up along the woods, and said, 'Now you wait.' We didn't know what we were waiting for, we didn't know where we were going, we only knew the azimuth on that old-school compass we needed to set." "It got dark, night fell. All of a sudden we’re standing on the edge of the woodline and they said, 'Go.' And we said, 'Where?' And they said, 'Follow your compass.' And that’s it. Figure it out." "So we set off individually, all knowing that our other colleagues were somewhere in the woods as well, but it was dark and it was summer, so it was hot and the trees were dense. And we set off, and at one point I noticed the ground below me kind of getting softer, noticed I was on a decline, and then I realized I was walking into a swamp." "And I kept sort of walking through. At what point in time is this not a good idea? In the dark, in a swamp, and there’s noises and crinkling, and I’m just staring at these little glow-in-the-dark lines on my compass." "And I said, 'Okay, if it gets to my knees, that’s maybe when I turn back.' And I kept going and the ground kept getting softer and mushier and mushier, and good thing I was wearing good boots. And I kept thinking maybe I should turn back." "But I kept going, we kept going, and that water only came up mid-shin and so I kept going. And I’ll be honest, trudging through a swamp in the dark with just a little glow-in-the-dark line on a compass, that was never a specific skill that I was going to need in real life. But the whole point of the training wasn't that destination." It was proving to myself, my colleagues, proving to themselves that—that you can do hard things and that quite literally you follow the compass, whether it’s the one in your hand or the one in your heart or the one in your mind, the compass leading you towards what it is that you wish to be doing. And so on your hardest days, wherever you are, whatever is challenging you, you will be able to draw on memories of things you have overcome, challenges you have faced. In my case, it’s the swamp. But yours might be a thesis you almost abandoned, a course that was particularly challenging, a semester that tested your limits, or an over-commitment to far too many activities where you wondered how you could handle it all. But Virginia Tech has been building these memories for you for four years. And that is part of what your diploma will represent: a record of the hard things that you have done, that you have gotten through, and places where you have stayed, following that inner compass because you are sitting here today. So take those memories with you. They are more portable than you think.
