Now that doesn't mean we'll have a solution by then. We hope we will, but we'll see. - I guess, switching gears to the LLM side, you mentioned some of this work you're doing with Tapestry, which I think would be really interesting for our listeners, so maybe just speak to that a little bit. - Okay, so this is kind of a little bit orthogonal to AME Labs. As if that wasn't enough to keep you busy. Well, it's a kind of an idea I've been forming over the last three years or so. It's the fact that people increasingly use AI assistants for various things, right. I mean, you see a decrease in the use of traditional search engines and you just ask a question to your favorite AI assistant. And, you know, if the plan that Meta and others are developing of having smart devices like smart glasses and stuff like that is realized, basically you'll just be talking to your AI assistant, you know, by voice with to your smart glasses or maybe some other smart device. And so all of your information diet will be mediated by AI assistants. If you are someone somewhere in the world, let's say outside the US or China, and you have an AI assistant and that AI assistant was built in California or Beijing or Shanghai or Shenzhen, it's not good for you. Like you may speak a language that those systems really haven't been trained to handle particularly well. You may have a culture that is not particularly well-understood by people in Silicon Valley and China, not well-represented by the training data that is publicly available on the internet. You may have a value system that is absolutely not represented by people building those models. And certainly, you will almost certainly have political opinions that are absolutely not represented by the handful of AI assistants you might be able to get from the West Coast tech companies or from Chinese companies. So what is the solution to this? Like how you serve a farmer in India or even a philosopher in France or Germany? And what you need is a platform which basically is an open, free foundation model, LLM-style, that is fine-tuneable by anyone to cater to the interest of people speaking a particular language, having a particular culture, having particular value systems, political biases, creeds, whatever it is. And so what you need is a wide diversity of AI assistants. There's a lot of countries around the world that are neither the US nor China who absolutely want some level of sovereignty for AI, not just for their industry but also for their citizen. They don't want their citizen to get brainwashed by a Chinese model or a Californian model, actually. And so they want sovereignty. How do you get that? So the way you get a platform like this, an open platform like this, to get to the frontier is you just train it on more and higher quality data than the proprietary systems. If you talk to people in India, in France, in Vietnam, in Morocco, in Switzerland, in Korea, Japan, Kazakhstan, everyone wants basically sovereignty. And you tell them like you guys have been training your model locally. You don't have to share your data, so that's the crucial aspect of Tapestry. You would have international contributors to Tapestry contributing to training a global model that would basically constitute a repository of all the world knowledge and culture if you want. But the contributors would contribute data and computing resources, but they would preserve the control on their data. They would not have to share their data with the other contributors. What they would contribute is parameter vectors, right. So it would be a kind of federated learning style thing where you have a bunch of data centers. They get the parameter vector from the global consensus of a model, think of it as an average of all the parameter vectors of all the contributors, right. So all the contributors periodically tell everyone else through maybe a central server, 'here is my parameter vector, what is yours?' Okay. And so you exchange parameter vectors like this and a local worker basically, whenever it updates its parameter vector, it tries to also make it as close as possible to the global consensus vector. So as the training of this thing kind of progresses, all those parameter vectors converge towards like a consensus model essentially, which is kind of a repository of all human knowledge. Now you have an open model that is as good as if it had been trained on all the data in the world. And now you can fine-tune it for your own purpose, your own political, cultural and linguistic biases or whatever you want, or centers of interest. I think there is a natural force for this to happen because most countries that are not the US nor China want sovereignty, but also because AI is fast becoming a platform and there is a natural tendency for platforms to become open. That's what happened with Linux, right, and that's what happened with the software infrastructure of the internet or the wireless network, it's all open source. It was proprietary initially, but that was all wiped out. - It's a really clever way to get around, you know, what seemed like this trend of decreasing open source. And obviously, I think there's been many fears that as the close source models get better, they'll be held back and they'll be used to train the next generation and you know they'll kind of be this almost like escape scenario for close source models where they get, you know, so much better than their open source counterparts. - So remember what, you know, who the big players of the internet infrastructure were in 1996: Sun Microsystems, HP, Dell, and a few others. Sun Microsystems was selling you Solaris with their, you know, proprietary hardware. HP with HPUX. They were claiming, you know, Unix is so much more reliable than Windows, you're not going to run a web server on Windows. Dell was doing this, you know, with Windows NT but like who is running Windows NT now as a web server? All of this was totally wiped out by Linux, like the entire internet runs on Linux. Even Azure, right, even Microsoft runs Linux. So basically OpenAI, Anthropic, etc. of today are the Sun Microsystems and HPUX of yesterday. - Yeah, I mean I guess implicit in that is obviously, you know, your view of like the limitations of what these models can only get so good and so it will be possible over time for the open source folks to catch up. - They've already run out of data, right. I mean the publicly available publicly available data, text data is already all used. I mean there is not more of it, right. So what those companies are doing is licensing commercial copyrighted data or training on synthetic data. - And I guess I'm curious because obviously there's been some impressive results in the last few years that they have been able to drive post these large-scale pre-trainings. IMO Gold, the MATH benchmark keeps going up. - Okay, that's very interesting. Now think about those two domains, math and code. Those are two domains where the language itself is the substrate of reasoning. It's not the only substrate of reasoning, but a lot of when you do mathematics, right, the formal way on a piece of paper, not the intuitive stuff but the manipulate language, right. And LLMs are really good at this. So proving theorems and stuff like that, that's what LLMs are really good at. They're not so good at the sort of coming up with like good concepts and definitions and things like that. It's more like 'here is a problem, solve it.' They're problem solvers. Mathematics is not just problem solving, right. Most of it is actually a creative act that those things don't do. And same for code. So LLMs are good programmers. They're not software architects. They're not computer scientists, right. But they can program for us. So they're not in a state where they can just replace humans entirely. It changes the world of humans, so humans now, you know, kind of go one level up in the abstraction hierarchy and our role is to decide what to build but like building it, you know, you can get help from LLMs. But okay, that's the important point is that LLMs are particularly successful at domains where the language itself is the substrate of reasoning, not for anything else. - What would an LLM like need to do to convince you otherwise? - I mean like a zero-shot agentic system, right. Have an agentic system, give it a new problem, it's not been trained to solve that particular problem, doesn't have a script for it. Is it going to be able to accomplish its task that it's never been trained to solve? And unless the system has the ability of predicting the consequences of its actions and then use that for planning, it's not going to be able to do it. And you're not going to do this with an LLM. You're going to do this perhaps with a significantly augmented LLM that is capable of search and planning blah blah blah. And currently LLMs that do math and code actually do this, right. Because they search for sequences of tokens that actually accomplish a particular task and they can run the code or verify that the proof is correct or whatever, so you have like a way of checking whether something that's produced is correct. But that's not a very efficient way of doing planning. And it only works in domains where this type of search can be performed in token space. What I'm talking about with JEPA is you don't do this in token space, you do this in, you know, abstract thoughts space. - And I'm sure some people listening might think, well you know hey, if it's inefficient and it works at things that are done in token space, that's still a large part of the economy. - Oh I mean if it works it's fine. I mean again there is nothing wrong with, you know, using LLM for what they're good at. It's just not a path towards human-level AI, you're missing, you know, an entire like a huge domain. - You seem like, hey it's going to tap out before it can become a software architect. - It's not going to tap out, it's just going to have like a limited, you know, ability to be deployed for a... it's going to become like increasingly difficult to deploy it for an increasingly large number of use cases because you're going to have to collect tons of training data for each of those use cases. And there is basically you're not going to be able to make those systems completely reliable, you know, without hallucinations or dangerous stuff or etc., unless those systems have the ability to predict the consequences of their actions, which means they're going to have to have explicit world models. - So a bet against the 100% accuracy and then also the generalization across different tasks. - Right. - One thing that's so interesting about the way the field has developed is obviously you shared the Turing Award with two others and I feel like they seem much more convinced of like maybe the power or potential threats or safety risks of LLMs over time. I'm wondering like when did your views start diverging? - In 2023. - And what like drove that in your mind? - I didn't change my mind. They changed their mind, okay. And just about the same time and it was basically GPT-4. I mean Jeff basically had was not connected to any of that, he was never really interested in LLMs, and discovered GPT-4 in 2023 when it came out and basically had an epiphany. He said 'oh my god those systems are really close to human-level intelligence and they have possibly they have subjective experience.' And he did a quick calculation saying, okay the human cortex has about 16 billion neurons. If you want to do something like backprop, okay the brain doesn't do backprop directly but if it does something like backprop like some sort of gradient estimation for some sort of objective function, you probably need a network of a few neurons to kind of reproduce the functionality of a virtual neuron in a neural net. And he said like let's assume, you know, maybe you need a circuit of 10 actual neurons to reproduce what a backprop neuron does. Then all of a sudden your cortex is only 1.6 billion neurons. Oh my god GPT-4 is really close to this. Okay so maybe it's gonna get as smart as humans. I do not believe in this claim at all. This is kind of, you know, Jeff's way of saying, 'okay basically I can retire, I can declare victory. I searched for the learning algorithm of the cortex all my career, maybe I didn't discover what it really was but backprop seems to be like a good substitute for it, it works really well and so maybe that's all we need. So I can retire and go around the world and give talks about the potential, you know, promises and dangers of AI.' That's basically what I think his intellectual trajectory has been. He's much less vocal about the potential dangers now than he was a year or two ago. He kind of realized there is probably a way to design true intelligent systems. First of all he realized that current LLMs are not that smart first of all, and second that there's probably a need for a few breakthroughs, conceptual breakthroughs, before we get to human-like intelligence. And third that the blueprint of those systems will be quite different from LLMs and we probably have a way of, you know, making them controllable and things like that. I've been saying this for years but okay, he sort of discovered this recently. Same kind of there's a similar thing with Yoshua. I think what they are both worried about is the ability of society and the political system to make sure that the benefits of AI will be maximized and AI would not, you know, just profit, make a few rich people even richer, and accentuate inequalities and cause major catastrophes because of bad usage. Okay this is not like the doomer scenario of AI taking over the world, it's more bad usage. - Which seems possible with the LLMs of today. - Which is a danger but I don't think it's as apocalyptic as what some people have claimed it is. Certainly not as apocalyptic as what even Anthropic has claimed and has tried to lobby governments into, you know, scaring government into regulating AI because of that. I don't subscribe to this at all. - They seem to genuinely believe it. - I think they genuinely believe it, but also I think there is some commercial good commercial reasons for them to believe that and to brainwash some people and government into thinking their systems are dangerous. - And it sounds like with these other architectures do you think... because obviously as bearish as you are on LLMs being the end state of everything, you have some pretty ambitious timelines too for these new architectures. It doesn't seem like you think we're particularly far away from some very compelling capabilities. How do you think about the safety around, you know, if these breakthroughs end up coming from newer architectures and whether that should make us rest easier or not? - I'm going to say something that again might be controversial and certainly some of my colleagues at Meta didn't like me saying this, but I think LLMs are intrinsically unsafe. I don't think they can be made reliable and safe. They cannot be made reliable because you can't stop them from hallucinating. And if they are agentic you cannot guarantee they're not going to take an action that they didn't predict the outcome of and that... - Does it surprise you they can do these like 15-hour coding tasks given the concerns around reliability? - Well coding is something where you can actually verify that the code that you generate satisfies your specification. But not everything is coding. And there are examples of, you know, coding agents like wiping up your hard drive, right. So or doing stupid things, right, that make you lose a lot of money or data or whatever. I think LLMs in their current forms are intrinsically unsafe because they cannot predict the consequences of their actions and because the way the task that they accomplish is determined is subject to their training. You give them a prompt and then they will accomplish a task that corresponds to that prompt only to the extent that their training has conditioned them to actually do the right task corresponding to this prompt. But there is no hard-wired constraint that will force them to accomplish this task and then predict that the task would be accomplished properly. Yeah, I mean I think famously in the early days right they would you'd ask them a question and they'd keep asking the question. Right, for example. Or they don't have common sense. I mean there's the joke that was circulating like a month ago of 'I need to wash my car and the car wash is 100 yards from my house, should I walk?' I tried it again like maybe two weeks ago, they all say 'yes you should walk' except Gemini. Gemini says... - So you think they're training on your video of having given that speech before? - It was not my video because I didn't come up with this example. But there are a few instances where I said like an LLM can't do this and then six months later it was capable of doing it and it's simply because as soon as people watch the podcast of me saying 'LLM can't do this,' they of course type it into ChatGPT. So now it becomes part of the training set and of course the next version has that in the fine-tuning set and of course it can answer the question. But it's not because it became smart all of a sudden, it's just because it was explicitly trained with that question. So LLMs are intrinsically unsafe. I don't think there is any way to fix that in the current paradigm. And what I've been proposing, the architecture I've been talking about, is Objective-Driven AI. So basically, you give an objective to an AI system which is 'accomplish this task.' Now how does the system know it will accomplish this task? It has a world model and it predicts the outcome of a sequence of actions it imagines taking, and if this outcome satisfies a cost function that describes to what extent the task has been accomplished or not accomplished, then that system, if the way that system works is by optimization, finding a sequence of actions that accomplishes this task, minimizes this cost according to its world model, it can do nothing else. And of course there's many things that can go wrong there. In particular, the cost function might be inaccurate. It could be that the cost function you think is actually measuring to what extent the task had been accomplished but perhaps it's not accurate. The world model might be inaccurate, so the prediction that the system makes is actually not the right one. So its prediction of what was going to happen as a consequence of its action wasn't right. So the system can still make mistakes, but it can predict the consequences of its actions to some extent, which is I think indispensable for any agentic system. Now what you can add to that system is not just a cost function that guarantees a task has been accomplished but you can also add a bunch of other objective functions, other cost functions, or even constraints that are safety constraints. They say, okay you know, don't hurt anybody on the way, right. And you can specify this at an abstract level but you can have low-level objective functions that put together will guarantee that the system will not be dangerous. And the system cannot violate those things, by construction it will have to satisfy those conditions. Not the case for an LLM. The LLM can always escape. There is a gap between your training error and test error, there is always going to be a prompt where the system is going to do really stupid things. - To talk through one specific space around LLMs, you're obviously really excited about AME and healthcare, and people have been using LLMs in healthcare for all sorts of things. I'm curious how you think about the set of things where LLMs are just not going to work in healthcare and you need like a model that understands the world better. - So designing a course of treatment for a chronic disease for example, or even a non-chronic disease for a particular patient which may not completely fit into templates that you've observed before. But if you have a good mental model of the dynamics of the physiology of the patient then you might design a course of treatment that will actually bring the patient to a good state. And when I'm saying a patient it can be a cell. Okay how do you tell a stem cell to turn into a pancreas beta cell that produces insulin? You have a patient with Type 1 diabetes and they have their immune system basically eats up their own beta cells, it's autoimmune. How do you keep making beta cells? You know can you send a message, do you have a model of a human cell that will allow you to figure out what sequence of message do you need to send to a stem cell so that it turns into a beta cell? - The less LLM-pilled camp and the LLM-pilled camp talk past each other, but I think it's actually very possible that both what LLMs can do which is maybe scaling what a top doctor, the treatment you get at a top doctor at a top place, going that around the world like unbelievable potential impact of that right, if you're able to do that. And then I think what you're talking about which is certainly still on the come for a lot of these things is well even better than the top doctor, like how do you go do that? - But it's more than just a top doctor, right. Because what a LLM can do well is, you know, it can sort of regurgitate knowledge that you can read in books mostly. But if medicine was only about accumulating declarative language that declarative knowledge that exists in books, you could be a doctor by just reading books. And you can't be a doctor by reading books. You have to do, you know, residency and actually kind of listen to the heart and press on the belly and things like that to, you know, diagnose appendicitis or whatever it is, right. Right? It's interesting. I will be very curious to see whether LLMs themselves can provide like top quality healthcare globally. We'll have to check back in on that one. It seems pretty close. - You know, I definitely want to also hit on your time at Meta because you spent over a decade building like one of the most respected research labs in the world. You recently left, as you reflect back on the time there, what do you think you got like most right and most wrong in your time running FAIR? - So the thing we got right is building a top research lab that really sort of innovated, produced a lot of the sort of basic methods and science and tools like PyTorch that are useful for the entire industry, right. I mean the entire industry is built on PyTorch basically, except for a few people at Google. And I think a culture of openness and scientific process is necessary for breakthrough innovation. Because there is a whole chain of innovation, right. You have blue-sky research and new concepts; a lot of that takes place in universities. Some of that takes place in advanced research labs in industry, which can be counted on the fingers of one hand. Google is a good one, FAIR was a good one—hopefully will still be, I'm not sure—and a few others. Then you have the next stage: "Okay, this is a good idea, let's push it forward and see if it can be made useful." This is still at the research level, in the sense that we're not going to fool ourselves. We're not going to try to just find a solution that just works for this problem; we're going to see if this technique that we imagine, or we picked up from other people in the community, can actually be pushed and be made practical. Not as a product, but we can show that it beats some record on some task or benchmark. And then the next stage is for the company that hosts the research lab to say, "Okay, now we're going to push the button, devote a big engineering effort to that vision, and then push it forward." That is where a lot of projects fail. That's where a lot of companies kind of fail to pick up. Meta was actually pretty good at this, but far from perfect. It was not like a textbook example of how you do it wrong, like Xerox PARC, where they totally missed out on the GUI interface, mouse, and windowing system. Meta just kind of missed a few steps, essentially. It's partly just organizational. It's partly because you need an organization that is pretty close to research, but not completely a product organization, to take the relay of pushing a technology a little further. You aren't making a product with a three-month deadline, but you are pushing things. We had that at one point at Facebook and Meta, and then we lost it. FAIR was basically isolated within the company and had lots of ideas that nobody picked up on. Then, in 2023, the GenAI organization was created by basically taking about 60 or 70 scientists and engineers from FAIR initially, and then it built up. But then it was under so much short-term pressure that, basically, that organization, GenAI, didn't have time to talk to FAIR. So, instead of being at the forefront and innovating in LLM, GenAI basically had to focus on short-term things and became very conservative. It's a combination of the modus operandi of the groups, but also pressure from the leadership. I mean, there's many ways things can go wrong, and you can't blame anyone in particular, but yeah, that's kind of what happened. - It feels like a lot of these organizations obviously are under short-term pressure right now because there's just an incredible race going on. And so I'm curious—obviously this FAIR setup you had, and there was a similar one at Google for many years, and certainly many researchers running around OpenAI and Anthropic trying many different things. Do you think that is still possible going forward, or is one of the only paths to leave and do your own company? Or are there still places within the industry that you think have this original ethos of FAIR, even amidst the race dynamics that are happening? - I think there are a few places within Google Research and DeepMind where people actually do research. But increasingly, the industry has become more closed, right. I mean, Google certainly clamped down, and Meta and FAIR even are kind of going a bit in the same direction. There are restrictions on publication now—like, more restrictions. And so it's less appealing for people who really want to do breakthrough research. They don't get as much resources if they do something that is relevant in the medium term, and they are told not to talk about it. So it's not a good atmosphere, I think, for breakthrough. It's not conducive. You know, the best way to get breakthrough research of the type that we were getting in the early days of FAIR, and at Bell Labs in the good days and Xerox PARC, is you hire the best people. Those are people who have a good nose to know what to work on and what projects to attack. You give them the means to succeed and you get the fuck out of the way. Pardon my French. - Yeah, I mean, I'm curious what impact that then ends up having on the broader research community. Obviously one of the legacies of FAIR is you trained so many researchers, right, and they're all throughout the ecosystem. And it feels like now the equivalents of those people that came in younger in their careers at FAIR, they're joining these labs with maybe shorter-term priorities and focus. I'm wondering, in this current ecosystem where it feels like a lot of younger people getting into the field are thrust much more into these short-term dynamics, does that change anything about the way the ecosystem evolves? - Well, I mean, the people who tend to want to work with me are generally people who are, you know, sufficiently crazy to do it. Or who kind of subscribe to the whole idea that in academia and during your PhD, you should work on the next generation of AI systems; you shouldn't work on the current generation. Like, if you work on LLM in academia now, it's incredibly boring. At least to me, it's boring. It's basically kind of studying how and why LLMs work and explaining why they work or what the limitations are. It's like descriptive science; it's really not very creative. I don't find that particularly interesting. It's useful. And, you know, if you really want to show how to do new things with LLM, you're not going to have the GPUs you need for that. So like, forget that. Don't work on LLM if you're doing a PhD. There's no point; you cannot contribute. - How did you know it was time to leave Meta? It sounds like you were thinking through some of these things over a period of time. Was there a moment that it crystallized? - Well, it was a combination of things, right. So first of all you have to understand, a lot of people have a completely wrong idea about what my role at Facebook and Meta was. So I joined in late 2013, really kind of started early 2014. The first four and a half years I was Director of FAIR, so I built the FAIR organization, set up the culture, hired the key people, and sort of managed it. And after four and a half years I stepped down from that role for a number of reasons. The reason is I was basically getting close to turning 60—first of all, 58—and I just don't want to do management. I mean, I was ready to do it for a while to get the organization started, but I'm just not good at it. It's not the thing I am. I'm more like a scientific or technical visionary and engineer and scientist. So other people are much better at management than I am. So I basically stepped down. Two other people, Joelle Pineau and Antoine Bordes, basically took over the directorship of FAIR and I became Chief AI Scientist. So I was reporting to the CTO and had roles of basically restarting a research project that I thought was necessary, because the ambition of FAIR was always to build intelligent systems, right. And I thought I had put my own research in parentheses while I was running FAIR; I just didn't have the time. And I thought it was important to basically design the architecture of human-level, human-like AI systems. You know, I had come up with the concept that this was going to be based on self-supervised learning and on prediction from sensory signals like video, things like that. I mean, these are old ideas. And world models—I actually gave a keynote at NeurIPS in 2016 where I said this is the way AI research should go. World models predict consequences of your actions and plan, and I said RL is not the thing that will take us there because it's too inefficient. Supervised learning has shown its limits, and so the future is self-supervised learning and world models. So how do we do self-supervised learning on world models? And I started a few projects on this with a few avenues that didn't pan out, some projects on video prediction and stuff like that. And then I came up with this concept that you could train self-supervised learning from video, but you have to train the system to make prediction in representation space. So that's the idea of JEPA. And if you have JEPA, you can turn it into a world model by making it action-conditioned, and then you can use it for planning. I had this idea around 2020 and in 2022 I wrote a long vision paper. So I said, "I'm just going to write a paper with my entire vision, okay, spill all my secrets, like I don't care." But maybe that will rally a bunch of people to that vision. And boy, did it work. Because not only did I rally a bunch of people—students who kind of came working with me at NYU or in Paris because they wanted to work on this—but also a whole team at FAIR who said, "This sounds great, that's what we want to work on." And then Joelle Pineau said, "Well, maybe this should be like a major mission of FAIR." We called it Advanced Machine Intelligence. That was the internal name of the project. And they let you leave with it. And now it's the name of the company. And you know, Mark Zuckerberg kind of read that paper and knew what it was about and subscribed to the project, and Andrew Bosworth the CTO also, and Mike Schroepfer the previous CTO also, and Chris Cox, who was my direct manager and Chief Product Officer, also loved the idea. So there was a lot of support in the leadership about this project that we internally called AMI. And it started really working for video. But then, the company kind of refocused all of its effort on LLM. Despite support from Mark and Andrew—Boz, we call him Boz—all the layers below didn't see the point, I think. And so politically, it sort of became a little difficult. The applications, as I said, of JEPA and world models are there. There are applications in wearable agents and stuff like that, and robotics. But Meta chose to get rid of its entire robotics AI group that was led by Jitendra Malik, who is now at Amazon. And so clearly it sort of wasn't the right environment anymore; most of the applications were in industry that Meta had no interest in. FAIR was increasingly getting pressure to basically help MSL with LLMs. So yeah, it became clear. And that throat-ramming worked really well with investors too, because when I had to raise money for AMI, everybody knew my story. Many investors and staff at various VCs had read my paper and had listened to my talks and had bought my story. They were realizing LLMs had limitations and were interested by the idea of building the next generation AI systems. - I guess, was the Scale acquisition part of this catalyst of the pure LLM focus internally? - Yeah, definitely. I mean, there's probably some other reasons to it; I don't have any sort of inside information to comment on this. But it's possible that Mark sees in Alex kind of a potential successor to himself, like a younger version of himself. - Yeah, I feel like a lot of the popular narrative in the media has been like, "Oh, when Alex comes in, it then gets harder to run a research organization." I don't know if that's to the extent you felt that. - Well okay, so here is a big misconception about my role, my relation to Alex, and how AI was run at Meta. I had zero technical contribution to LLaMA—none whatsoever. My one contribution to LLaMA was to argue for open sourcing LLaMA-2. Because there was a big internal debate whether we should open source. Like, the legal department was against it, the policy department was kind of against it, the comms department was for it, all the engineering side was for it, Boz was for it. So there was enormous internal discussion at a very high level—40 people from Mark Zuckerberg down every week for two hours for months. So really, it was a big debate internally. And I really argued for open-sourcing LLaMA-2 because the safety risks were basically overblown, and the opportunities to create an industry were extremely strong. We were going to jump-start the AI industry by open-sourcing LLaMA-2. And in fact, that's exactly what happened. But I had zero contribution to LLaMA, positive or negative. Like, I didn't do anything to stop it or slow it down or anything. There were a lot of people working on LLMs within FAIR and that was fine. I never said anything against it, other than saying this is not a path to human-level intelligence, but it's fine and it's useful. It is the same thing for speech recognition or translation, right. And particularly since 2018, when I stepped down from being Director of FAIR, I didn't have any direct influence on what people were working on other than basically publishing my vision and then rallying people around my project. But they were working with me because they wanted to, not because I was their boss. I wasn't telling them to work with me. And so I had no positive or negative influence on LLM within Meta. I had some influence on the strategy, but it was more like the long term and how you maintain a research lab and things like this. And in the last year—starting maybe early '24 and certainly in '25—the way FAIR was moved and managed basically did not correspond to what I thought was necessary to preserve innovation research and breakthrough, and to preserve the good people. A lot of good people have left already. - Yeah, and I guess for a lot of people probably it was harder to get people to work on the stuff you were working on internally, and I'm sure there was pressure for you yourself to work on a lot of this LLM stuff. - Yeah. No, but a lot of other people also have left. - No, it's fascinating. I mean, one thing I'm struck by throughout our whole conversation is I feel like you've had a remarkably consistent point of view for a long time. You can go back to a bunch of the earlier talks you referenced. Obviously, it is a fast-moving space and a ton of interesting things have happened in the last year. What's one thing you've changed your mind on in the last year? - Oh, I mean the whole idea of what we used to call unsupervised learning that we now call self-supervised learning. You know, until about 2003, the whole idea of unsupervised pre-training was that you get a good representation for the input data and then you either fine-tune the model with a little bit of supervised labeled data. And it sort of gives us some evidence that this whole technique could work. I tried to apply this to video because, ultimately, what I wanted to do is train a system to understand how the world works by just watching the world go by, right. I mean, that's the basic idea. And I sort of started to argue for this in the early 2010s and did some work on simple video prediction—we didn't have GPUs, okay. And then I started doing this more seriously after the creation of FAIR by doing pixel-level video prediction. I realized that wasn't working, but then I started arguing for self-supervised learning. This whole idea is of training a system generically, not to solve a task, but to basically just predict, and then using the representation that is learned this way as input to a downstream task that you can train supervised or with reinforcement or whatever. That was a bit of the topic of my second half of my keynote at NIPS in 2016—it was still called NIPS at the time in 2016. And then I kept pushing for this idea and tried to discover some methods to get that to work. And what surprised me is that that became incredibly successful, but not for video—for language. LLMs basically are a blindingly successful example of self-supervised learning. - That they are. Well, I feel like that's almost like the perfect note to end on, but I want to make sure to leave the last word to you. I feel like all our listeners are very familiar with you, but I want to at least give you the mic to point them to anything that you think they should check out with some of the new stuff you're doing. The mic is yours. - Okay, let me tell you one thing. An LLM works because when you have a sequence of discrete symbols, making predictions is easy. There's only a finite number of possible symbols in your language—100,000 possible tokens or something like that—and you can have your neural net produce a probability distribution over all possible tokens. Then you can sample from that distribution, shift the token into the input, and then produce the next token and do auto-regressive prediction. Okay, so that's a special case. If you have the real world, you can't use a generative model. So now you have to train a system that learns a representation and makes prediction in the representation space. There's a big issue with this, which I didn't think was easily solvable until about five years ago, even though I invented one technique to solve it decades before that. And it's the problem that if you take two inputs—let's say the initial segment of a video and the continuation of that video, or you take one image and a corrupted version of it—you run them both through an encoder and you train a predictor to predict the representation of one from the representation of the other. There is a very simple solution where the system basically predicts a constant representation and now the prediction problem becomes trivial. That's called a collapse—representation collapse. So the big question of self-supervised learning for JEPA—for those joint embedding architectures—is: how do you prevent collapse? The solution that I came up with many years ago in 1993 is contrastive learning. So basically, you have examples of things that should be predictable from one another and examples of things that should not be predictable from one another. It turns out this method works, but it doesn't scale with dimension; it doesn't scale very well. There's another set of methods that are called distillation methods, and they do prevent collapse. We don't know why. A good example of that is DINO, or Dino. That's a joint embedding method using the distillation method. Basically one of the encoders trains the other one; it is used as a teacher for the other encoder. And the encoder that is being trained, you do backprop to it. The one that is not being trained, you don't do backprop, but you share the weights with the other one with some exponential moving average. It's a collection of recipes. There was a paper from DeepMind about it called BYOL, Bootstrap Your Own Latent, which uses this trick. That trick is derived from some intuition from reinforcement learning and somehow it prevents collapse, but we don't know why. There's a few theoretical papers on it that explain why it possibly might work in some simple cases, but it's not satisfactory. The cost function you think you're minimizing, you're not actually minimizing, and so you can't monitor it. It actually goes up when you train. So we don't like this method. But it works. And some of the models we've trained—large-scale video representation learning system V-JEPA, V-JEPA-2, v2.1—they trained using this method, I-JEPA also. But we're moving away from this. And now we have a few papers that came out recently on an explicit regularizer to prevent this collapse, which basically tries to maximize information content coming out of the encoder. It's in the same family as Becker and Hinton from '89 and Schmidhuber 1992 and a bunch of others since then. To some extent, contrastive techniques also do this, although it's not sample contrastive. And then the question is, how do you measure information content? How do you maximize the information content coming out of a neural net? The problem is if you want to maximize a quantity, you either need to be able to measure it or you need to have a lower bound on it. For information content, we only have upper bounds. We cannot measure it; we can only come up with upper bounds. So we take an upper bound and we cross our fingers. And it kind of works. The latest one is called SIGREG, that means Sketch Isotropic Gaussian Regularization. We had a previous one called VICREG, or VicReg—Variance Invariance Covariance Regularization. And the SIGREG stuff is really cool. This is some work by Randall Balestriero, who was a postdoc with me and is an Assistant Professor at Brown now. It basically consists in forcing the distribution of variables coming out of the encoder to be joint Gaussian, essentially to maximize information if you want. It's just a very different way of doing it than what Jurgen Schmidhuber and Sue Becker and Jeff Hinton were doing. And so this is super promising, in my opinion. And we have variations of it—one that can produce sparse representations, another that can produce isotropic representations but not necessarily Gaussian. And we have a paper with Randall and a student at Mila, Lucas Mais, where we train a world model with this. It's still small-scale, but we think it's super promising. So if you want to read one paper, read that paper: it's "Le World Model," L-E World Model. - Awesome, we'll definitely link to it too. - Yeah. I'm not responsible for the name; Randall picked the name. - Amazing. Well Yann, seriously, thank you so much. It is just such a privilege to get to spend the last bit of time with you and really appreciate you coming on the podcast. - All right, thanks for having me. That was fun. I'm Jacob Effron and this has been Unsupervised Learning—a podcast where I get to talk to the smartest people in AI and ask them tons of questions about what's happening with models and what it means for businesses in the world. As I hope is clear, I have a ton of fun doing this. It's a nights and weekends project in addition to my day job as an investor at Redpoint. But our ability to get these incredible guests on really comes from folks like you subscribing to the podcast and sharing it with friends. It's really what ultimately makes this whole thing work. And so please consider doing that and thank you so much for your support in listening. See you next episode.

TL;DR

President Trump and President Xi’s high-stakes Beijing summit yielded pledges from China to assist in reopening the Strait of Hormuz and a major Boeing aircraft deal, while the U.S. simultaneously engaged in critical "aid diplomacy" with a crisis-stricken Cuba.

The Beijing Summit: Global Security and Stability

  • Iran and the Strait of Hormuz: Secretary of State Marco Rubio confirmed that President Trump pressed China to use its influence as Iran’s largest ally to stabilize the region.
      • Military Pledges: President Xi reportedly pledged that China would not provide military equipment to the Iranian regime.
      • Strait Access: China expressed opposition to the "militarization" of the Strait of Hormuz and the implementation of an Iranian tolling system for commercial vessels.
      • Nuclear Red Line: Rubio emphasized that the U.S. position remains firm: Iran cannot be allowed to acquire a nuclear weapon, which would give them permanent leverage over global energy corridors.
  • Taiwan Tensions: Despite the "partner, not rival" rhetoric at the state dinner, Chinese officials warned that the relationship could "collide" if the Taiwan issue is mishandled.
      • U.S. Stance: Secretary Rubio stated that U.S. policy remains unchanged and the U.S. will continue to defend Taiwan’s status, though China prefers a "voluntary" unification.
  • Human Rights: President Trump raised the case of Jimmy Lai, the pro-democracy publisher currently imprisoned in Hong Kong. The U.S. is seeking his release and would be open to him relocating to America.

Economic Deliverables and Trade

  • Major Manufacturing Deals: The administration is touting significant "wins" for American jobs, most notably an order for 200 Boeing jets.
      • Agricultural Gains: Commitments were made for increased Chinese purchases of U.S. beef and soybeans.
      • Energy Exports: There is growing Chinese interest in purchasing more U.S. oil and gas to replace or supplement Iranian supplies.
  • CEO Diplomacy: The summit featured a high-profile "all-star" business delegation including Tim Cook (Apple), Jensen Huang (Nvidia), and Elon Musk (Tesla/X).
      • Access: Chinese Premier Li Qiang told the CEOs that "China’s door will only open wider" to American business interests.

Diplomatic Crisis and Engagement in Cuba

  • High-Level Negotiations: CIA Director John Ratcliffe met with Cuban officials in Havana to deliver a message: the U.S. will only engage on economic issues if "fundamental changes" are made to the regime.
  • $100 Million Aid Package: The U.S. offered $100 million in food and medicine to combat the island’s total collapse of the electrical grid and fuel supplies.
      • Distribution Conditions: Rubio specified that the aid must be distributed by non-governmental organizations, such as the Catholic Church, to ensure it reaches the people rather than the government.
      • Regime Acceptance: After initially calling the offer a "lie," Cuban leader Miguel Díaz-Canel officially posted a statement accepting the aid.

Domestic Briefing and General News

  • Hantavirus Monitoring: The CDC is monitoring travelers from an infected cruise ship. While one initial test was a false positive, officials are keeping high-risk individuals in quarantine; current public risk is considered "low."
  • Alex Murdaugh Legal Twist: The South Carolina Supreme Court overturned Murdaugh’s double-murder convictions due to jury tampering by a clerk of court. A new trial has been ordered.
  • Missing Person Investigation: The U.S. Coast Guard seized the "Soulmate" vessel 40 miles off the Florida coast. The boat belongs to Brian Hooker, whose wife, Lynette, vanished in the Bahamas weeks ago.
  • Panda Diplomacy: Two new giant pandas, Fu Shuang and Ping Ping, are being prepared for transfer from Chengdu to Zoo Atlanta as part of a 10-year conservation agreement.

Special Report: Trump and Xi give closing comments during U.S.-China summit

NBC News(11.9M)·29:59·241.0K views·2026-05-15
Donald TrumpPresident of the United States Xi JinpingPresident of China
TL;DRPresident Trump and President Xi concluded a high-stakes summit in Beijing, signaling a shift toward diplomatic stability and "fantastic" trade prospects—including potential multi-billion dollar Boeing and energy deals—while navigating deep-seated tensions over Taiwan and Iran.

TL;DR

President Trump and President Xi concluded a high-stakes summit in Beijing, signaling a shift toward diplomatic stability and "fantastic" trade prospects—including potential multi-billion dollar Boeing and energy deals—while navigating deep-seated tensions over Taiwan and Iran.

Diplomatic Atmosphere and Symbolism

  • Venue Significance: President Xi hosted the meetings at the Zhongnanhai compound, the historic residence of Chinese leadership since 1949.
      • The choice of venue was a reciprocal gesture for the hospitality Xi received at Mar-a-Lago in 2017.
      • The leaders engaged in "tea diplomacy" and toured the grounds, which feature trees up to 1,000 years old.
  • Personal Rapport: President Trump characterized the relationship as "stronger than ever before," describing Xi as a "friend" of nearly 12 years and a leader he "respects greatly."
  • Reciprocity: A follow-up state visit was announced; President Xi is scheduled to visit the United States on or around September 24th.

Trade and Economic Commitments

  • Major Trade Deals: Trump claimed "fantastic trade deals" were reached that would benefit both nations, though specific contractual details were not immediately released.
  • Aerospace and Energy:
      • Boeing: Trump suggested China may purchase 150 to 200 Boeing jets, though earlier reports speculated the number could reach 500.
      • Energy Exports: Discussions included China purchasing oil and natural gas from Texas, Louisiana, and Alaska to help the U.S. become a dominant global energy player.
  • CEO Delegation: The summit featured a "powerhouse" business delegation intended to project U.S. economic strength and secure market access.
      • Nvidia: CEO Jensen Huang emphasized his focus on representing U.S. interests and supporting the President.
      • Apple: CEO Tim Cook cited a goal of "great collaboration" between U.S. tech and Chinese markets.
      • Tesla: Elon Musk attended high-level meetings with Chinese Premier Li Qiang.

Geopolitical Security and Regional Stability

  • Iran Nuclear Stance: Both leaders agreed that Iran must not be allowed to possess nuclear weapons.
      • Strait of Hormuz: There is a mutual interest in reopening the straits to ensure the flow of commerce and energy.
      • Military Equipment: Trump claimed Xi committed to ensuring Chinese companies do not provide military equipment to Iran, despite reports of potential third-party "loopholes."
  • Taiwan Tensions: President Xi used the summit to issue "frank and stark" warnings regarding Taiwan, which China views as a domestic issue.
      • Arms Sales: Tensions remain high as the U.S. Congress has approved $14 billion in arms sales to Taiwan, currently awaiting the President’s signature.
      • Semiconductor Factor: Analysts noted Taiwan’s role as the "world capital of microchip production" remains a critical commercial subtext for both nations.

Strategic Outlook and Risks

  • Projecting Stability: For China, the summit’s primary goal was to project global stability and validate Xi's status as a global statesman.
  • Fragile Progress: Analysts warned that despite the cordial "chocolate cake" moments, the relationship remains adversarial and fragile.
      • Historical precedents, such as the 2017 summit, show that trade wars and volatility can quickly override diplomatic goodwill.
  • Media Environment: The summit was highly controlled; Chinese state media emphasized mutual cooperation, while avoiding the "rogue moments" or aggressive press questioning typical of White House briefings.
"We've made some fantastic trade deals, great for both countries. [Xi] is a man I respect greatly, become really a friend... we've settled a lot of different problems that other people wouldn't have been able to settle." — President Donald Trump
"This visit is a historic and landmark visit. So far, we have reached a new bilateral relationship, which is a constructive strategic relationship. This can be said to be a milestone visit event." — President Xi Jinping
"We discussed Iran. We feel very similar on Iran. We want that to end. We don't want them to have a nuclear weapon. We want the straits open." — President Donald Trump

Things can change very quickly depending on what happens in the geopolitics of the world. Yet very quickly after that, trade tensions emerged, the pandemic emerged, and this relationship turned very frosty. So I think there needs to be caution moving forward. And while again there are these exchanges of goodwill and good feeling, I go back to a conversation I had with the former U.S. ambassador to China just a few months ago while I was still in Beijing. And again he made it clear, this is still very much an adversarial relationship, and that reality can't be ignored in this present time. And again, moving forward as we see perhaps a meeting of these two men scheduled for September here in the United States, Frances. All right, Marc Stewart, former Beijing correspondent and global business strategist, thank you. And we will be back here live with you as soon as President Trump emerges from that closed-door summit. And as always, we have much more ahead on NBC News Now and online at nbcnews.com. I'm Frances Rivera, thanks so much for watching. We thank you for watching, and remember, stay updated on breaking news and top stories on the NBC News app or watch live on our YouTube channel.

Inside the Mind of a Tech Investor with Gavin Baker

Sohn Conference Foundation·25:00·2026-05-15
Gavin BakerFounder, Managing Partner & CIO, Atreides Management Jas KhairaHead of Blackstone N1
TL;DRThe artificial intelligence transition is a historic, physical capacity cycle constrained by silicon and power limitations rather than a typical speculative bubble.
Investors must focus on hardware-level execution, such as Amazon's underestimated Trainium chips,...

TL;DR

The artificial intelligence transition is a historic, physical capacity cycle constrained by silicon and power limitations rather than a typical speculative bubble. Investors must focus on hardware-level execution, such as Amazon's underestimated Trainium chips, specialized neo-clouds, and the long-term shift toward space-based orbital compute.

Gavin Baker’s Investing Framework and Philosophy

  • The Fidelity DNA: Baker maintains the core Peter Lynch philosophy that deeply engaging with products as a consumer is the best way to understand a stock ("if you like the store or product, you'll love the stock").
      • The Contrarian Challenge: He struggles to balance this with a natural tendency to over-focus on low valuations and contrarian positions, noting that "pulling weeds and watering flowers" (selling losers and riding winners) is a lifelong discipline.
  • Formative Mistakes and Leverage: Baker highlights his early investment in Nextel International as a painful, defining lesson in risk management.
      • He publicly pressured the board to buy back stock 15 months before the company went bankrupt, teaching him to be highly wary of high leverage in competitive markets.
  • Information Edge: Baker prioritizes reading primary source materials, transcripts, and expert network transcripts over meeting with public company executives. He notes that public executives rarely deviate from official filings, and "reading is much faster than they can speak."
  • The "Lightning Round" Framework:
      • *Underrated:* Pattern recognition, sleep.
      • *Overrated:* Scuttlebutt.
      • *Appropriately Rated:* Position sizing (knowing whether you play for "slugging percentage" or "batting average" and staying consistent).

The Semiconductor Bottleneck and Avoiding an AI Bubble

  • A Unique Capacity Cycle: While historical memory cycles suggest selling memory stocks now (with Micron's margins in the high 60s versus a 16% historical average), Baker believes this mirrors the mid-1990s capacity cycle where a structural shortage extended gains far longer than anticipated.
  • TSMC as the Safeguard Against a Bust: The typical tech cycle bubble-and-bust may be prevented by physical constraints imposed by Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Company (TSMC).
      • The TSMC Mentality: TSMC's leadership, whom Baker describes as "flinty old men and women in their 70s," acts as the conservative custodian of Morris Chang's legacy.
      • Rate-Limiting Nvidia: Despite Nvidia's Jensen Huang wanting to double or triple wafer capacity, TSMC expands capacity conservatively (around 5% at a time), enforcing a physical ceiling on the market that prevents oversupply.

AI Monetization, Usage Pricing, and "Token Maxing"

  • The Shift to Usage-Based Pricing: Frontier AI companies are transitioning from $250/month flat subscriptions to enterprise usage-based pricing with overage costs. Baker compares this to the early growth days of cellular and long-distance telecom, where overage charges unlocked massive customer lifetime value.
  • Coding as the Ultimate App: Code generation is not just a killer app; it is the shortest path to Artificial Superintelligence (ASI) and Artificial General Intelligence (AGI).
      • Tools like Claude Code and Code X are highly recommended even for non-coders seeking high-quality investment and analytical answers.
  • Under-Penetrated Market: Currently, only 10 basis points (0.1%) of the world's population uses AI models at a cutting-edge level. The cumulative spend of trillions of dollars is already causing shortages, indicating astronomical demand when adoption reaches even 5%.

Hardware and Infrastructure Winners: Trainium and Neo-Clouds

  • The Rise of Trainium: Amazon’s Trainium 3 (ramping up in the second half of 2026) is the most underestimated chip in the market.
      • Architectural Advantage: Google made highly conservative design choices with its TPU V8 (and refuses to submit TPUs to its own MLPerf benchmarks). Conversely, Nvidia and Amazon made aggressive choices.
      • Mixture of Experts (MoE) Scale: Nvidia and Amazon's Trainium possess the only two functioning "switched scale-up networks" globally, which are required to run inference on modern MoE models.
  • The Durability of Neo-Clouds: Specialized cloud providers like CoreWeave, Crusoe, and Nebius have highly durable business models, rejecting the idea that they are mere arbitrage plays on hyperscaler capacity.
      • The Formula 1 Analogy: Managing GPU clusters is exceptionally complex. High-quality neo-clouds achieve two to three times higher GPU utilization per hour than lower-tier providers, justifying premium pricing.

The Future of Infrastructure: Orbital Compute

  • Timeline to Orbit: Space-based "orbital compute" will become commercially viable and proven within the next two years, gaining meaningful market share by the end of the decade.
  • Disruption of Terrestrial Ecosystems: While existing physical data centers will always hold value for training and reinforcement learning, the shift to space will heavily penalize land-based power, cooling, and industrial equipment companies that have over-expanded capacity.
  • Mechanics of Space Computing: Space-based setups bypass terrestrial power grids and cooling challenges.
      • The Setup: Rather than building massive warehouses, orbital compute uses sun-synchronous satellites designed as 8-foot-tall racks stitched together via lasers. Power is drawn directly from solar wings, and cooling is achieved by positioning giant (300 to 400-foot) radiators in the shadow of the rack.

Notable Quotes

"Based on every memory cycle we have had for the last 25 years, this is the time to be selling memory 100%... However, there's one cycle where you absolutely do not want to sell. And that's the cycle we had in the mid-90s, which is the last true capacity cycle... we may still be very early." — Gavin Baker
"They [TSMC leadership] are the custodians of this legacy... they're just simply not expanding capacity as fast as Jensen wants... I think these flinty old men and women who are safeguarding Morris Chang's legacy are helping us all avoid a bubble by enforcing a real-world physical constraint." — Gavin Baker
"Running one of these clusters is like driving a Formula 1 race car. You watch Formula 1 races, and it looks easy... But if I tried to get in a Formula 1 car and drive it in a race, I would die... All GPU hours are not the same." — Gavin Baker
"Coding may be the shortest path to ASI and AGI. Because if you can write code to do something and write it for yourself... that's a pretty fast, elegant path." — Gavin Baker
Our guest today named his hedge fund Atreides, which for the non-nerds in the room means "the spice must flow." And for those of you who remember spice from your freshman year in high school, it's not that kind of spice. For Gavin, spice is high-bandwidth memory. His Arrakis is Taiwan, and his sandworm is Jensen Huang. His X bio reads, "No investment advice, views my own." This may be the most expensive disclaimer in modern finance because he's got 288,000 followers who follow his advice anyway. He ran $17 billion at Fidelity and beat 99% of his peers—although he just told me it's actually 100% of his peers. And he now runs his hedge fund from Boston, which may be the most contrarian position of all. Ladies and gentlemen, please welcome the most knowledgeable man alive on semiconductors, Gavin Baker. Wow. Thank you, and let me introduce you, Jas. You don't have to do that. Jas is one of the most senior partners at Blackstone, and he just took over a new job heading all of Blackstone's AI strategy. He was formerly the head of their tactical opportunities strategy. We've been friends for a while, so it's fun that he gets to interview me and I get to interview him. But Gavin, I do get to interview you. Since this is an investing conference, I wanted to start with your mental model as an investor before we get into the meat and potatoes of semiconductors, memory, the stack, and the bottlenecks, which I'm sure all of you, just like me, want to engage with you on. So, you started at Fidelity in 2000 and managed it through two bubbles. What is one piece of Peter Lynch-era Fidelity DNA that you've kept, and what is one that you had to break in order to start, run, and be successful with Atreides? Yeah, good question. I'd say that Peter Lynch DNA is forever woven into me, as I suspect it is woven into anyone who had the great fortune of starting their career at Fidelity, which was an amazing place for me. It is this concept that if you like the store or you like the product, you're going to love the stock. Just the importance of engaging with new products and companies as deeply as you can as a consumer and user—that for sure is embedded in me. What I have been working on my entire professional career as an investor is dealing with a stock market axiom. Peter Lynch said you want to "pull up your weeds and water your flowers." You want to sell your losers and ride your winners. For whatever reason, that is very hard for me. I'm extremely valuation-sensitive, I'm very contrarian, and I'm most comfortable on the 52-week low list. I've been hanging on to the memory stocks for dear life. Yeah, that has been a lifelong journey that I try to get a little better at every year. Most great investors point to a single bad trade as the formative one in their own journey. What was yours? And what specifically does it stop you from doing today that you would otherwise do? Well, I would say there are two. I had a really hard year in 2011 or 2012, and it involved two stocks. One was a company called Accredo Health, and they were helping, effectively, small businesses and small hospitals do a better job of negotiating with big insurance companies. You know, you feel like it's a good cause. And then you wake up and there's a front-page, 10,000-word article in the New York Times on Sunday about how they're denying care to people in need, which was simply not true. Nonetheless, the stock went down 90% to 95% and never recovered. The lesson is that there are always unknown unknowns. There are always risks present, no matter how much you try to dimensionalize an opportunity. And the second one is embarrassing. It was Nextel International. I was a telecom analyst in the past, and for a long time, you could make a lot of money in telecom. Whenever a new network was built in an emerging market, it was definitionally the lowest-cost, best, and highest-quality network because it wasn't loaded. Nextel International built a new network in South America. It was the latest and greatest 3G network, and they were migrating off something called iDEN. I'd made money in this kind of pattern many times, so I made it a large position. I'll never forget writing a letter to the board insisting they do a buyback. The company was bankrupt 18 months later. The lesson is to be very, very careful of high leverage because the company was just too levered. Sometimes not everything goes right. What happened was a price war broke out between two unrelated competitors who were much larger, and they got caught up in that. I'll forever have the black mark of having written my only letter to a board asking them to buy back stock just 15 months before the company went bankrupt. It was a good lesson. Yeah, I appreciate that. Thanks for the candor and the vulnerability. We'll mix it up a little bit. Sure. Usually, lightning rounds come at the end. Great. Put it at the beginning. Overrated or underrated: Pattern recognition? Scuttlebutt? Position sizing? Sleep? You just get one word. I think pattern recognition and sleep are highly underrated. I think scuttlebutt is overrated. What was the fourth? Position sizing. Position sizing is very important. You just have to pick your game and play it. You can either be a slugging percentage player or you can be a batting average player. You have to know what your game is, stick to it, and be consistent. Appropriately rated, sounds like. Let's look at the production function of Gavin Baker. When you actually look at where your edge came from, maybe this year or last year, what fraction is reading? What fraction is your network—maybe not so much given the scuttlebutt? What fraction is just being early to one or two correct frameworks? I think reading is overwhelmingly the most important part of it. I will admit I no longer, or very rarely, meet with public companies. I only meet with them if they want to meet with me, and they're just very well trained. They never say anything that's not in a transcript or a 10-Q, and I can read much faster than they can speak. Because of that, I read a vast amount of transcripts and primary source material. I also think these expert transcripts are very good and a great use of AI. You know, when Nvidia reported that great quarter in May of 2023 that kind of kicked everything off, ironically six months after ChatGPT had come out, I think a majority of hedge funds didn't employ a semiconductor analyst. Semis are a lifelong love for me, as is deep tech, but I would say now people have gotten really smart on semis. Yeah, and you were so early to it. That's a good segue because of that idea of a framework and your mental model. Today, memory prices are up 60%. Micron's margins are probably in the high 60s, whereas the historical average was closer to 16%. You've been talking about the compute shortage broadly, the shortage stacking into data centers and power, and extending into leading-edge wafer capacity. What is your mental model for how this evolves? You've also said that whenever there's a shortage, there's eventually a glut. How does this evolve? Talk us through that. Well, that has been true throughout history, and I'm sure there will eventually be a glut, but "eventually" is doing all the work in that sentence, not "glut." I would say, based on every memory cycle we have had for the last 25 years, this is the time to be selling memory, 100%. I was actually the Micron analyst in the year 2000, and I remember going to their analyst day in Sun Valley. I'm a veteran of many, many memory cycles, and based on history, this is the time to sell. However, there's one cycle where you absolutely do not want to sell, and that's the cycle we had in the mid-90s, which is the last true capacity cycle that I would argue we've had in memory. Based on that cycle, we may still be very early. I listened to my friends Alex and Leon, who I thought did a great job, as did Leslie earlier, and I would just say I take the over on every number that they gave. Every single number. I think that they're conservative guys, and I bet they would take the over too. Nobody wants to be wrong a year later here at Sun. So, we may still be early. This may be the first true capacity cycle, and I do think that these fundamental shortages are good for us as investors. The last thing anyone should want is a bubble. Bubbles are terrible. They're awful, they are terrible to invest through, and the aftermath of them is even worse. We don't want a bubble. And unfortunately, the entire history of financial markets suggests that whenever you have a profound new technology—whether it's AI, the internet, the PC, railroads, or canals—you almost always get a bubble because markets are efficient. Investors understandably become excited about this new technology. As Michael Mauboussin frames it, there's a breakdown in diversity. Everyone comes to believe in this. You get a bubble, and then that bubble funds the build-out that the new technology required. That's exactly what happened with the internet. I am optimistic that we may avoid a bubble this time. Smoother for longer is what we all want. The reason we're going to avoid it is we have fundamental shortages of watts and wafers. We're going to address the watt shortages with orbital compute, for sure, in the next five to seven years. But the wafer shortage, I think, is going to persist for a long time. The reason it's going to persist for a long time is Taiwan Semi is run by flinty old men and women in their 70s. Not to say 70s is old—it's the new 50. I'm 50, and it's the new 30. But they're the most important people in Taiwan. The president of Taiwan is irrelevant; they are Taiwan. And they view themselves as the guardians of Morris Chang's legacy. I remember going to Science Park in Taiwan more than 20 years ago and asking, "Do you think you could ever catch Intel?" They said, "It's a beautiful dream, but it's probably for our grandchildren." And they did it in one lifetime. So, they're the custodians of this legacy. They need to preserve Taiwan. A Taiwan bubble and bust is a disaster for Taiwan Semi and for Taiwan. Because of that, they're simply not expanding capacity as fast as Jensen wants. Jensen goes there every three months, and maybe they expand 5%. He wants them to double or triple. If they doubled or tripled the capacity, Nvidia could probably sell $1 trillion, $1.5 trillion, or $2 trillion worth of chips next year. I really believe that. But the other side of that might be very painful for everyone. So, I think these flinty old men and women who are safeguarding Morris Chang's legacy are helping us all avoid a bubble by enforcing a real-world physical constraint that simply has not been present in past technology cycles. Yeah, no, it's fascinating. As a monopolist provider, they're rate-limiting supply at some fundamental level. They dismissed Sam Altman as a "podcast bro" after they met with him. You and I were talking about this backstage, which is that you would take the over on OpenAI and Anthropic combined at $200 billion of revenue in maybe 12 to 18 months—I don't want to put a strict timeframe on it, but near term. It turns out that code generation was the killer app to monetize AI, at least in chapter two of this whole journey. If we're going to go from where we are today to get to that $200 billion in the next 12 to 18 months, where is that revenue coming from? Is every S&P 500 company going to miss earnings because of token spend on Anthropic? I don't think that is an edge case. If you are not aggressively token maxing, you should be. But point number one: all of these models are shifting to usage-based pricing. That is no longer true. The best capabilities are locked behind harnesses and reserved for people on enterprise plans who can pay for use. Now, this is wildly bullish for AI. It's incredibly bullish for the pricing of these frontier tokens. Going back to the cellular industry, the reason cellular was a great industry for a growth investor, and the reason long distance before that was a great industry, is that you bought a fixed amount of minutes, and then if you went over, you paid by the minute. People really liked to talk to their friends and family, and that was why telecom was a great growth industry for a long time. We're just moving from these "all-you-can-eat" plans to usage-based plans with overages, where those usage tokens cost a lot more. We're finding out that we're nowhere near the ceiling price for how much people will spend. So, I think you'll get a lot of productivity. I think this fundamental compute shortage we have highlights what Alex said, which was so profound: 10 basis points of the world's population is using these models the way they should probably be used, and we're in an insane shortage despite spending cumulatively trillions of dollars.
