You will get better answers for investment questions, even if you're not a coder, than you will using the regular model. Yeah, no, I appreciate that. You talked about silicon and what's happening on the chip side. Obviously, you were very early in Nvidia. I mean, you were talking to Jensen in 2000, let alone 2023 when the ChatGPT moment happened. Alternatives are coming. Competition is coming. He's still going to be the dominant part of the market, but competition is coming. As you think about Trainium, TPUs, and MTIA, which of these is most underestimated by the market? Where does the consensus get it wrong? Trainium, by far. Trainium is going to be to 2026, especially in the second half of this year when Trainium 3 really ramps, what TPUs were to 2025. If somebody is wildly bullish on TPUs today, let's go look at their 13F and see if they owned Momentum or Elastica, which were the best ways to invest in TPUs. I owned one of those, so I feel like I have some credibility to say this, but I do think Google, for a lot of reasons, made very conservative design choices with the TPU v8. Nvidia and Trainium made very aggressive design choices, and so Trainium is for sure the most underestimated. This is not only because of those design choices, but because all of these frontier models are what are called mixture of expert models. To inference one of these, not to get too technical, you need something called a switched scale-up network. The only two functioning switched scale-up networks in the world today are the ones that power Nvidia's GPUs and Amazon's Trainiums. This is why Google invented the MLPerf benchmark, yet they will not submit TPUs to their own benchmark, which you can see is visibly driving Jensen crazy. But listen, the TPU is a great chip. I'm sure the TPU v9 is going to be amazing, and they'll make more aggressive choices. I would never bet against Google, and I would never bet against Broadcom. But I do think Trainium is super underestimated right now. Yeah, I appreciate it. I want to switch gears to a topic which actually is how we first got connected way back in 2022 and then 2023: neo-clouds. I called you in the summer when you were on vacation somewhere to bug you about a company called CoreWeave in the summer of 2023 and get your advice and input. Ultimately, that led to us investing $7.5 billion behind CoreWeave to scale them up at a really pivotal moment. So, first of all, thank you for that. Fast forward to today: CoreWeave, Crusoe, Nebius, Lambda, etc. Is the category durable today? Is it a transitional arbitrage on hyperscaler CapEx and token friction? I absolutely think it's durable. First of all, CoreWeave is a little bit of a sad subject for me. Atreides could have invested over $50 million in the round at a $1.1 billion valuation, but I was conflicted out by Crusoe. I love Crusoe, I think Crusoe's going to work, and we have a large position in Crusoe, but every time I think of being able to put $50 million into CoreWeave at a billion dollars, I get a little sad. But I'm very happy you put $7.5 billion in. It's absolutely a durable category. The way to think of running one of these clusters is like driving a Formula 1 race car. You watch these Formula 1 races and it looks easy, like anybody could do it. It's the same way if you watch Tom Brady, you think, "Well, why did he miss that throw?" Well, because he's in a stadium with 100,000 people yelling, and there's a bunch of people who weigh 100 pounds more than him running 20 miles per hour at him trying to kill him before he throws the ball. In Formula 1, it's the same thing. It looks easy, but it's really hard. To put it in perspective, if I tried to get in a Formula 1 car and drive it in a race, I would die. I would be a danger to myself and a danger to everyone, including the people in the stands. That is what running a cluster is like. It's really hard to do well. The reason a company like CoreWeave can charge a huge premium for their GPU hours is because all GPU hours are not the same. Those CoreWeave GPUs are being utilized two to three times more per hour on average than GPUs from a more bottom-of-the-barrel provider. By the way, this all goes for Crusoe, Nebius, and other high-quality neo-clouds. I think this is very underappreciated, and people think, "Oh, that can't be a durable competitive advantage." Well, I was a retail analyst in the year 2005, and I observed that all you had to do to create a $50 billion market cap in America in any category was to be able to run 1,000 stores in 50 states with different climates and different consumer preferences, staffed by friendly, knowledgeable employees who don't steal from you, stocked with the right inventory at the right time at the right prices, in clean, well-lit stores. That's it. In history, maybe ten companies have been able to do that. Running one of these clusters is even harder. So, I think it is durable. For a long time, the hyperscalers were stuck in a cost mentality. The hyperscalers were competing with people running these Formula 1 cars, and they were doing overnight shifts in 18-wheelers trying to stay awake to deliver the lowest cost. That's not what AI is about. Now, I think they're making this mental and cultural shift, and they've made it. But I think some of these neo-clouds have a very durable business model. Yeah, we certainly agree. We've talked a little bit—and you've actually alluded to it already tonight—about orbital compute. We're not going to get into the science of it because there are papers you can read online and run through your favorite LLM like Grok to distill. When does it become commercial in a way that it gains meaningful market share? Orbital compute—when does that commercially gain real share, and what is your view on the most underappreciated short in the market today that is not being priced in? Is it terrestrial data center operators? If that's the case, we're in a lot of trouble at Blackstone. But are there other things that today are clear shorts for you based on that? I think it becomes clear that it is possible, functional, and economical in the next two years. I think it starts to take meaningful share towards the end of this decade. I do think there may come a day where we never build another terrestrial data center, though the ones that have been built and are in the ground are always going to be valuable. You're going to do training and RL in terrestrial data centers. But I can imagine a day in the next seven years when we never build another terrestrial data center. The years leading up to that are going to be very painful for a lot of the companies in the power, cooling, and real estate sectors—these industrial names who have massively flexed up capacity to support a build-out that could really come to a screeching halt. In space, the power comes from the sun, and the cooling comes from the dark side of the satellite. There are certain names that I'm not supposed to say, but if you've seen the illustrations of a prominent potential provider of orbital compute and what their satellites are going to look like, the radiator is 300 or 400 feet long. It sits in a sun-synchronous orbit, literally in the shadow behind the satellite. So, you have these big solar wings, the satellite itself, and the satellite is just a rack—not a whole data center, just a single rack eight feet tall, two and a half feet wide, and four feet deep. They're stitched together using lasers to make a virtual data center, and then you just have the radiator in the shadow behind the rack. Gavin, we're out of time, but thank you. This has been fantastic. I appreciate you doing this. Thank you. Thank you all.

Extended Interview: Tom Llamas sits down with Secretary of State Marco Rubio

NBC News(11.9M)·22:10·424.0K views·2026-05-15
Tom LlamasAnchor and Correspondent, NBC News Marco RubioSecretary of State Xi JinpingPresident of China
TL;DRSecretary of State Marco Rubio, attending the Trump-Xi Summit in Beijing, emphasized a "strategic stability" approach to China that combines direct diplomatic engagement with a rigorous domestic policy of re-industrialization to reduce reliance on Chinese supply chains.

TL;DR

Secretary of State Marco Rubio, attending the Trump-Xi Summit in Beijing, emphasized a "strategic stability" approach to China that combines direct diplomatic engagement with a rigorous domestic policy of re-industrialization to reduce reliance on Chinese supply chains. While reporting progress on agricultural and aerospace trade deals, Rubio reaffirmed unwavering U.S. positions regarding Taiwan’s status quo and the necessity of preventing Iranian nuclearization and maritime interference.

U.S.-China Relations and Economic Strategy

  • Diplomatic Realism: Rubio defended his presence in China despite being previously sanctioned by the country, stating his role as "chief diplomat" requires direct dialogue between the world's two largest economies and militaries to manage "irritants" and prevent conflict.
  • Trade Agreements: The Secretary confirmed new agreements for China to purchase American products, including:
      • Aerospace: Boeing airplanes and jet engines.
      • Agriculture: Significant agricultural purchases (details pending from the Trade Representative) and potential future energy exports.
  • Re-industrialization: A core priority is bringing key industries back to the U.S. to counter China’s goal of dominating future markets.
      • Critical Supply Chains: Rubio cited lithium batteries—an American invention now dominated by China—as a primary example of what the U.S. must reclaim.
      • AI and Semiconductors: While the U.S. remains dominant, Rubio stated it would be "stupid" to sell cutting-edge chips to China that could be reverse-engineered.
      • Electric Vehicles (EVs): Rubio characterized Chinese EVs as a national security threat, arguing that allowing them into the U.S. market would "wipe out" the domestic auto industry and its secondary components.

Regional Security and the Taiwan Question

  • Policy Consistency: Rubio asserted that U.S. policy toward Taiwan remains unchanged following the Trump-Xi meeting, despite President Xi emphasizing Taiwan as the "most important issue."
  • Status Quo: The U.S. maintains that any forced change to the current status quo would be globally "problematic."
  • Military Build-up: Rubio noted that the Chinese military's growth over the last decade is "unprecedented," particularly its navy, signaling ambitions to project global power similar to the United States.
  • Reunification: While acknowledging China’s preference for a "voluntary" reunification, Rubio warned that any attempt to use force would result in global repercussions.

Middle East Stability and Iran

  • Straits of Hormuz: Rubio stated that China and the U.S. find common ground in opposing the militarization of the Straits and Iran's attempt to charge "tolls" for the international waterway.
  • Military Standing: Rubio claimed that after "Project Epic Fury," the traditional Iranian navy is "completely gone," leaving them with only small speedboats and drones.
  • Diplomatic Efforts:
      • The U.S. is pushing for a UN resolution (sponsored by Bahrain) to diplomatically isolate Iran and reopen the Straits.
      • Project Freedom: This military operation was paused at the request of Pakistan to allow for a diplomatic solution, though Rubio noted internal divisions within the Iranian regime are stalling negotiations.
  • Nuclear Position: Rubio reiterated that the U.S. will not allow Iran to acquire a nuclear weapon, a stance he believes China fundamentally shares, even if they are less active in enforcement.

Cuba and Humanitarian Aid

  • Aid Stalled: The State Department has offered $100 million in food and medicine to Cuba, but Rubio accused the Cuban regime of lying about the offer's existence.
  • Distribution Conditions: The only "string attached" to the aid is that it must be distributed by non-governmental organizations, such as the Catholic Church, to prevent government theft.
  • Long-term Goal: Rubio stated the U.S. plan is to foster a "prosperous future" and prevent Cuba from remaining a "failed state" 90 miles from U.S. shores, though he expressed skepticism that the current leadership is open to necessary economic changes.

Mr. Secretary, we thank you so much for your time. Thank you. Thank you. I do want to start by asking you, are you surprised that you're even here in China? As a US Senator, you know you were sanctioned by China because of what you said talking about China's record when it came to human rights abuses. You have never shied away from that. Do you still feel the same way now that you're in this country? Yeah, I mean, the difference is my job now is no longer just to be a senator. My job is a different job. I'm the chief diplomat of the country, and I execute on the president's foreign policy. The second thing I would say is, irrespective of that, even in my time in the Senate, I would always acknowledge that the United States and China have to have a relationship. It's the two largest economies in the world, and probably the two most powerful militaries in the world. And I think it's irresponsible for us not to have direct dialogue with them. In areas where we can find mutual cooperation, I think we can—there's probably virtually no problem in the world that we can't solve if we work together on it. But with two big, powerful countries like this, there's always going to be irritants. There's always going to be areas of disagreement. It's our job, for the sake of our respective countries but ultimately for the sake of the world, to try to manage those areas of difference. You know, they've got red lines and things they never want to talk about, and we have them as well. Our job is to do and promote the policies that are in the best interest of the United States. So for our viewers at home, there's so much going on in the world, and the world is watching what's happening here. What's the main takeaway you can tell our viewers at home? What was negotiated, what's been decided, and how is it going to change your lives back in America? Well, first and foremost, I mean part of the problem we faced in the United States for a long time is we've allowed our nation to be de-industrialized. If you think about factories, manufacturing, and all kinds of key industries that have left the country, lithium batteries—that's something America invented and China now dominates that market. So I always said it, and I always said it when I was in the Senate: China's doing what I would do if I were Chinese and I were a Chinese leader. They are trying to dominate the world in all these key industries of the future. We may not like it, but that's what they're going to do because they're acting in their best interest. We have to act in our best interest. I'm the Secretary of State of the United States and before that, as you pointed out, a senator for the United States. I work for the president of the United States. Our job is to promote the interest of our country and to bring back as many of those industries as possible. So the areas we're always going to be talking to them about are unfair competition, you know, their control of critical supply chains—so many critical supply chains that we are reliant on, but not just us, the world. It is overly reliant. So I think what you'll see as a result are two things. There is some agreement: they're going to buy more of certain things which is important, like American airplanes and American jet engines. But I think the other thing that's important for us is to keep up the work of re-industrializing America, bringing back to the United States those industries that are key to us. The Chinese aren't going to like it because they want to dominate those industries, but that's what's good for the American people. The second point is just that engagement between the presidents of the respective countries is critically important. They were able to talk about the Straits of Hormuz, we were able to talk about North Korea, and we were able to talk about all these other parts of the world where China plays a role as well. So just to nail down specifics here, we're talking about the purchasing of airplanes—Boeing airplanes—and then also there's been some reports about beef as well. Is there anything you can shed light on that? Well, I think the details will be announced later today. I don't want to get ahead of the actual announcement by the trade representative. But there's going to be some agricultural purchases which are important, and they're important for our growers. China needs those things. We hope in the future to expand it to energy purchases. You know, the United States is a net over-supplier of energy now to the world, one of the world's—if not the world's—largest energy suppliers at this point. Obviously, when it comes to the airplanes and the engines, those are American factories and American workers that are making that. Anytime you can gain access to a market as large as this one, that's a very positive thing. But there's a lot of work yet to be done. The CEO of Nvidia is here with you guys. He traveled with the president on Air Force One. Were AI chips discussed at all? In the context of the president's conversation with President Xi, no, we didn't get into that level of specificity. We know what that is. I mean, at the end of the day, the United States remains a dominant player in the space of AI and in the space of semiconductors and chips. That said, the Chinese are investing billions and billions of dollars in their own industry. So that's going to be an area of competition. Do you think AI chips should be sold to China? Well, I think as you've seen, the president's announced that certain chips can be sold. Obviously, it's up to the Chinese to accept it. But I think we always have to establish our area of dominance—in essence, the cutting edge, the things that give us an advantage. It would be dumb and stupid for us to sell that to anybody, especially a country that's going to reverse engineer it and leapfrog us. So I think that's understood. Maturely speaking, there are things the United States still has a dominant position in, and from our national security perspective, we should keep that dominance. We want to keep that dominance. The Chinese are going to do their best to catch us and even surpass us. We shouldn't make it easy for them the way we kind of did for 20 years when we sort of had a different view of this relationship. Yeah. I want to read you something. Your counterpart, the Foreign Minister, has put out a statement which you may have seen or you may not have seen—I want to make sure you hear about it. He says, quote, "Taiwan independence and cross-strait peace are as irreconcilable as fire and water. Safeguarding peace and stability across the Taiwan Strait is the biggest common denominator between China and the US." And again, the context of all this is that President Xi stressed to President Trump that the Taiwan question is the most important issue in China-US relations. Talk to me about that moment when that was discussed. Well, they certainly feel that way and they always raise that issue, and we understand they raise that issue. From our perspective, any forced change in the status quo and the situation that's there now would be bad for both countries. One of the things the Chinese emphasized, which we agree with, is strategic stability in our relationship. A constructive relationship, but also one that establishes strategic stability so that we don't have misunderstandings that could lead to broader conflict. And so we always reiterate the point: we hear them when they say this. We always respond by saying anything that would compel or force a change in what we have now would be problematic. We would certainly—our policies on that have not changed. It's been pretty consistent across multiple presidential administrations and remains consistent now. Did President Xi request to President Trump not to sell weapons to Taiwan? Well, that topic may have been discussed in the past. It did not feature prominently in today's discussion. We know what their position on that is already. Remember, Congress plays a role in that process as well. And we have sold them weapons in the past; that's existed as recently as December, which they were very upset about. That's a decision the president gets to make as Congress appropriates and as Congress decides what to do with those topics. We will respond accordingly. But nothing has changed in the way the US views the relationship with Taiwan. It will defend Taiwan as has been—yeah, US policy on the issue of Taiwan is unchanged as of today and as of the meeting that we had here today. It was raised. They always raise it on their side. We always make clear our position and we move on to the other topics. We know where they stand and I think they know where we stand. Do you think China wants to invade Taiwan? Well, I think China's preference is probably for Taiwan to willingly, voluntarily join them. In a perfect world, what they would want is some vote or a referendum in Taiwan that agrees to fold in. I think that's what they would prefer. Ultimately, it's featured prominently in President Xi's mandate in the time he's been in office. He's made clear that what they call reunification—that's what they call it—is something that has to happen at some point. We think it would be a terrible mistake to force that through force or anything of that nature. There would be repercussions for that globally, not just from the United States. And we kind of leave it there. That sort of ambiguity is what I think has defined our way we characterize this issue. And the reason being is strategic ambiguity. We don't want to see conflict. We don't want to see something disruptive happen because I think it would be very disruptive for the world and for both countries. I know you're watching China. You don't think they're ramping up their military to do something in Taiwan? Well, I think they're ramping up their military in general. Not—I mean, the pace of growth in the Chinese military over the last 10 years has no precedent. None. I mean, just what they've done with their navy alone over the last—they've put billions and billions and billions of dollars in their system. So it's, you know, you look at it, I mean it's hard to ignore how fast and how big. So I don't think it's just limited to Taiwan. I think they have ambitions to ultimately be able to project power globally the way the US does now. They're not—they're still behind us in that regard, but nonetheless they are investing a lot of money. They are right now the world's second most powerful military without a doubt. Let's talk about Iran. Did President Trump raise the issue of Iran with China? He did, and it was important because the Chinese side said they are not in favor of militarizing the Straits of Hormuz and they're not in favor of a tolling system. And that's our position. We don't—we will never support an Iranian tolling system in the Straits of Hormuz, nor do we think they have a right to put mines in international waters. And so it's good that we have alliance or at least agreement on that point. I think the fundamental question is: what are we going to do about it? We have a resolution now before the United Nations in which 100 and something countries have cosigned. The Bahrainis are the sponsor, but we're strongly behind it and have been pushing very hard on it. It very clearly makes those points. So we hope the Chinese will vote for it. Right now we haven't gotten their commitment to vote for it yet at the United Nations. Maybe that'll change after today's meeting, I don't know. Can you help me understand: what exactly did President Trump ask President Xi for when it comes to Iran? He didn't ask him for anything. And to make it clear so they understand because—I mean, it's logical we would talk about it given how dominant that issue is. Our position is very clear: Iran cannot have a nuclear weapon. And that's what they were, you know, they were trying—they were on the verge of building a conventional capability where they had so many rockets and so many drones that you couldn't do anything against them. They would hide behind that conventional shield to do whatever they wanted with their nuclear program in the future. That's why the president chose to act. In response to that, Iran has decided that they're going to take an international waterway and turn it into theirs and charge tolls for it. Wait, they oppose exactly what? They oppose what Iran is doing. They want to set up a system where ships have to pay Iran to use an international waterway. And now they've gotta do something about it. They should vote for our resolution at the UN. You know, for our viewers back home in America, every day Americans are spending more and more. And they're saving less and less. How do you explain to them why is this taking so long in Iran? Well, first of all, I would say obviously it may seem like a long time from the perspective of other conflicts that we've seen in the past. It's only been weeks, not months and certainly not years. Understand what I'm saying by this. If you have 20,000 missiles, you can't possibly shoot down 20,000 missiles. I mean, it would overwhelm your systems. Once they establish that point of immunity, not only could they hold the world hostage with a nuclear program, they could hold the world hostage with the straits.
